This report was written by Mauricio Carrizosa (task manager) based on an appraisal mission that visited
Brazil from November 30 to December 20. The mission also included Rodolfo Salcedo (Financial
Management), Clemente del Valle and Hoon Sahib Soh (debt management institutions), and William
Dorotinski (the PPA). Anne Pillay provided final processing logistics and editing of the report. The report
also drew upon the work of the concurrent mission that reviewed Brazil's Multiyear Planning framework
(the PPA), headed by Yasuhiko Matsuda, and that also included Geoffrey Shepherd and German Rios.






BRAZIL
PROGRAMMATIC FISCAL REFORM STRUCTURAL ADJUSTMENT LOAN
LOAN AND PROGRAM SUMMARY
Borrower:         Federative Republic of Brazil
Implementing      Ministry of Finance and Ministry of Planning and Budget.
Agencies:
Poverty Category:  Not Applicable.
Amount:           US$757.58 million (including financing of the front-end fee). This is the
first of two, and possibly three loans, totaling $1.2 - 1.5 billion and
supporting a program of fiscal reform actions.
Terms:            Fixed-spread loan in US dollars, with a repayment period of 12 years
including five years of grace.
Commitment Fee:  0.85% per annum during the first four years and 0.75% per annum
thereafter on undisbursed loan amounts, beginning to accrue sixty days
after the loan agreement is signed.
Front-End Fee     1% of the loan amount, to be financed under the loan.
Objective:        Strengthen the foundations for improved growth, poverty reduction and
public service delivery by improving (i) aggregate fiscal performance
through incentives for fiscal responsibilities, and (ii) the use of public
resources, through better allocation and technical efficiency of
expenditures, including better service delivery to Brazil's citizens and
improved debt management.
Description:      The Program consists of series of actions undertaken during a two year
period 2001-2002 to imnprove fiscal performance. The actions cover
implementation of the Fiscal Stability Plan and the Law of Fiscal
Responsibility (LFR); upgrading of the federal financial management
system, development of the federal multi-annual expenditure plan (the
Plano Pluriannual or PPA) framework, completion of administrative
reform, and implementation of a program to bring Federal debt
Management institutions closer to best practice.
Benefits          Benefits include: (i) continued aggregate fiscal balance; (ii) improved
fiscal planning, transparency, and performance incentives, including
stronger incentives for sub-national fiscal adjustment and
creditworthiness; (iii) an improved federal financial management system,
(iv) improved incentives for civil service performance; (v) a long term
expenditure framework (the PPA), closely linked to the budget, achieving
a more transparent and better allocation and technical efficiency of
expenditures, and strengthening national/subnational and
government/private sector partnerships; (vi) improved delivery of public



services to Brazilian citizens; and (vii) quality of debt management raised
to international best practice for large open economies.
Risks               *  External or domestic shocks with an adverse bearing on domestic
interest rates, growth, and fiscal performance. This risk is mitigated
by the high probability that, as with previous shocks, the government
will take effective macro/fiscal measures to manage shocks;
*  Weak enforcement of LFR with an adverse effect on fiscal planning
or fiscal execution. This risk is mitigated by Brazil's demonstrated
high commitment to fiscal sustainability and by the positive
contribution of the LFR to an orderly fiscal planning and budget
execution process that will help sustainability;
*  Weak management of PPA process, resulting in a weakening of the
PPA approach. This risk is mitigated by the Government's
demonstrated commitment to the new PPA approach, by
institutionalization of the PPA at the constitutional level, and by the
usefulness of the approach to plan and manage budget priorities;
*  Inadequate consensus on completion of administrative reform, with
an adverse bearing on personnel costs, on personnel management,
and on the quality of service delivery. This risk is mitigated by other
possible measures to contain personnel costs, including continued
restraint on employment and wage growth.
Financing Plan     Not Applicable
Net Present Value  Not Applicable
Project            PE-PO-60575-LEN-BB
Identification
Number



1
MEMORANDUM AND RECOMMENDATION OF THE PRESIDENT OF THE
INTERNATIONAL BANK FOR RECONSTRUCTION AND DEVELOPMENT TO THE
EXECUTIVE DIRECTORS ON A PROPOSED PROGRAMMATIC FISCAL REFORM
STRUCTURAL ADJUSTMENT OPERATION TO THE FEDERATIVE REPUBLIC OF
BRAZIL FOR BRAZIL'S FISCAL REFORM PROGRAM
INTRODUCTION
1.    This memorandum proposes a Programmatic Fiscal Reform Structural Adjustment Loan
(PFRSAL) to the Federative Republic of Brazil. The loan would be the first of two, or possibly
three loans to support an ongoing program of fiscal reforms covering fiscal sustainability,
expenditure management, and debt management. The second or third one-tranche adjustment
operations would be proposed to the Board once further progress has been achieved in the
reforms under the program. The reforms supported by the loans would be completed during
2001-2002. The proposed first loan would be US$757.58 million. It follows the Fiscal and
Administrative Reform Special Sector Adjustment Loan approved in March 2000, which
supported initial implementation of the Government's fiscal stability plan, approval and
implementation of Administrative Reform, and the refinancing of the state's debts.' The
proposed loan is preceded by extensive analytical work, including periodic fiscal monitoring, a
fiscal sector report2, a debt management report3 and active policy dialogue on debt management
issues, and ongoing work and dialogue on Brazil's new multi-annual budget planning practices
and on financial management4. The proposed loan is being complemented by a Programmatic
Financial Reform Structural Adjustment Loan. Both structural adjustment loans will be
complemented, in turn, by related Technical Assistance (TA) operations, with the fiscal reform
TA loan covering possible assistance in the four areas of the PFRSAL, namely fiscal,
expenditure, financial management and debt management.
2.    The objective of the Program is to strengthen the foundations for improved growth,
poverty reduction and public service delivery to citizens by improving Brazil's fiscal
performance through continued efforts at attaining fiscal sustainability, improved allocation and
efficiency of public sector expenditures (including better delivery of public services to citizens),
and improved efficiency of debt management. Improved performance in these regards will help
reduce poverty, through three main venues. First, aggregate fiscal adjustment will help reduce
real interest rates, increase growth, with higher growth accelerating poverty reduction. Second,
better use of public resources will increase the impact of public expenditures on the welfare of
' The follow up loan was originally envisaged as a second special sector adjustment loan which would support the
new Fiscal Responsibility Law and the approval of pending legislation regarding administrative reform. As the
emergency conditions that prompted the Bank's special assistance have now subsided, support for additional reforms
is being proposed at the Bank's standard terms.
2 The World Bank, Structural Reform for Fiscal Sustainability, Report No. 19593 BR.
3 The World Bank, Brazil - Public Debt Management, Report No. 19207-BR, June 30, 1999.
4 The World Bank, Brazil - Multiyear Plan: PPA 2000-2003, draft; and 0 Proceso Orcamentario Brasileiro
Relatorio Preliminar



2
the poor. And third, special public expenditure programs are targeted to improve the quality in
the delivery of public services in general, with public services (e.g. health and education) largely
targeted to the poor. The first loan in the program would support measures and actions that have
already been enacted and are being implemented, including Brazil's Fiscal Stability Program, the
Fiscal Responsibility Law, the reform of Brazil's federal multi-annual expenditure planning and
management process, and institutional reforms in asset liability management. The next one or
two loans of the programs would support further actions in these areas. The expected outcomes
from these measures and actions include (i) a sustained overall public sector fiscal balance that
will allow the ratio of public sector net debt to GDP to remain stable or decline; (ii) improve
fiscal planning, transparency and performance, including stronger incentives for sub-national
fiscal adjustment and creditworthiness; (iii) a long term expenditure framework (the PPA),
closely linked to the budget, achieving an increase in the strategic content of expenditures
(including partnerships of the federal government with sub-national governments and of
governments with the private sector), as well as in their outcome orientation, transparency and
efficiency (both in terms of their allocation across programs as of their results in each program);
(iv) an improved federal financial management system, (v) improved incentives for civil service
performance; (vi) an improvement in the quality of public service delivery to Brazilian citizens,
including better services to the poor; and (iv) an increase in the efficiency of debt management.
3.    These changes help consolidate the major transformation that Brazil's public sector has
been experiencing over the last several years in order to become more effectively strategic and
results-oriented. The changes reflect a vision whereby the public sector should move from a
bureaucratic state to a managerial state, with a focus in the areas where it can be effective. This
movement is sharply reflected in the reforms supported by the proposed operation: a Fiscal
Responsibility Law that steers the public sector towards planning and management of fiscal
objectives; a programmatic approach that focuses expenditure management to the attainment of
results with a bearing on the welfare of Brazil's citizens; an improvement in debt management
institutions that seeks to provide the accountability and the capacity to effectively manage
Brazil's large public debt; a reform to make the government's financial management system
responsive to the government's managerial requirements in those areas. This movement also
makes the public sector more likely to contribute to the country's growth and poverty reduction.
By ensuring fiscal stability and a strategic approach to expenditures, Brazil's public sector will
better encourage private investment, private sector synergies, and public sector coordination to
raise economic activity. The Bank's partnership with the Government with regard to these
important changes is at the core of the 2000 CAS, which envisages adjustment lending in support
of structural fiscal reforms and, most specifically, improvements in fiscal institutions and public
sector management for better fiscal performance and incentives for fiscal discipline.5 The
remainder of this memorandum discusses Brazil's fiscal reform program in detail and the
proposed PFRSAL.
5 See Brazil, Country Assistance Strategy, March 6, 2000 (Report No. 20160-BR), p. 31.



3
RECENT ECONOMIC DEVELOPMENTS
MACROECONOMIC PERFORMANCE AND POLICIES
4.    Since the early 1990s, Brazil has been engaged in a major reform of its economy, moving
away from import substitution and state-owned enterprises towards greater emphasis on private
initiative, competition and international integration. Considerable progress has been made in
controlling inflation and creating an improved economic environment for private enterprise,
fiscal adjustment and reform of the public sector. From 1992 to 1997 Brazil experienced a very
successful growth performance. Annual growth averaged 4.2%, a major improvement over the
average for the previous five-year period (0.0%). Factors contributing to this positive outcome
include a trade liberalization during the early 1990s and a well-designed and implemented
economic stabilization program initiated in mid-1994 (the Real Plan), together with a favorable
international economic environment. By contrast, the two and a half years from mid- 1997 to end-
1999 saw a much more volatile international economic environment with a series of crises in
international markets. These crises adversely affected growth in Latin America and Brazil was
no exception. The average rate of growth during 1998-99 was only 0.3%. However, by the end of
1999, recovery, led by the industrial sector (particularly consumer durables and capital goods),
was firmly established. GDP growth rates between the last quarter of 1998 and the last quarter of
1999 and between each of the first two quarters of 1999 and the first two quarters of 2000
averaged 3.9%, close to the 4.0% consensus estimate of Brazil's likely average growth over the
next several years.
5.    As a result of the stabilization plan introduced in 1994, annual inflation declined from
over 2000% in 1993 to less than 2% in 1998. Even the large currency depreciation of January
1999 had a fairly limited small pass-through impact on inflation. Inflation in 1999 was contained
to about 20% on broad price indices and around 8% on consumer price indices. This was the
result of sluggish aggregate demand and continued monetary tightness. Following the change in
the exchange rate regime in 1999, from a crawling band to a floating rate, Brazil introduced a
sophisticated inflation targeting framework. Targets were set at 8%, 6%, 4% and 4% respectively
for the IPCA index duringl999-2002, with a margin on each side of two percentage points.
Inflation in 1999 was 8.9%. Inflation during the 12-month period through November, 2000 was
6%, which is exactly the mid-point of the governments 2000 target. In its September 2000
inflation report, BCB expected an inflation below 4% for 2001 (somewhat lower than the
market's median expectation in September of 4.3% for 2001). With an improved fiscal
framework and assuming an absence of external shocks, the inflation targets for 2001-02 remain
realistic. These targets provide the anchor for the Government's monetary policy.
6.    Fiscal policy performance has also improved considerably. As early as 1995, the
Government attempted to address the structural fiscal deficit unmasked by price stability.
However, lack of legislative consensus meant that fiscal adjustment lagged behind other parts of
the economic reform program. As a result, Brazil has been dependent on large capital inflows.
This dependence coupled with the prevailing exchange rate regime implied vulnerability to
sentiments in international markets. The Government responded competently and effectively to
the loss of market confidence in late-1998 and early-1999 with a combination of tightened
monetary policy and fast-acting fiscal adjustment measures coupled with a new drive for
structural fiscal reforms. Throughout 1999 and 2000, fiscal adjustment has been carried out with



4
tremendous effort and remarkable discipline and is achieving the ambitious targets that were set.
These measures, taken together with the shift to a floating exchange rate in January 1999 have
been successful in preserving economic stability and regaining market confidence. Provided the
external environment remains benign, Brazil is now approaching steady, non-inflationary
growth. Brazil's remaining economic vulnerability results from its continuing, though declining,
fiscal and current account deficits. As fiscal deficits are closely correlated with current account
deficits, fiscal adjustment at both national and sub-national levels are critical for continuing to
reduce vulnerability and restore growth.
POVERTY REDUCTION
7.    Following a period of increasing poverty from 1987-1994, the Real Plan significantly
reduced poverty levels during the period 1994-1998. The national headcount ratio (using a
poverty line of R$65 per capita per month at 1996 prices) declined from 34% in 1993 to 22% in
19986. This beneficial effect was due to: (i) economic growth and rising real wages; (ii) the end
of inflation (salary erosion and inflation tax); (iii) a significant appreciation of the local currency
shifting relative prices toward the service sectors in which most of the poor work; and (iv) a
significant increase in the minimum wage to which important income sources, such as pensions,
are tied. Despite these advances, Brazil continues to be a country with high income inequality
and a high level of poverty for its level of per-capita income. The average 1998 headcount ratio
of 21.7% masks important regional differences, with regional headcount ratios ranging from
8.5% in the South East to 47.3% in the North-East.
8.     The succession of economic crises since 1997 has affected the poor mainly through
reduced labor demand and the resulting reduction in employment and/or real wages.
Nevertheless, since economic performance in 1999 was much better than feared at the time of the
devaluation in early 1999 and key social expenditures have been protected, the negative poverty
impact of the crisis was much less severe than expected. According to available measurements of
labor incomes 7, the headcount poverty rate in six metropolitan areas dropped from a peak of
35.3% in July 1994 to a low of 24.6% in October 1997. The rate increased continuously from
November 1997 until March 1999, when it reached 30.0%. The latest data through 2000 shows a
reduction in poverty rates indicating a reversal in the negative crisis impact. Country-wide
indicators show a trend of slowly reducing labor income inequality. The rate of open
unemployment reached a high of 8.2% in March 1999 and has since fallen to 6.8% (October
2000). Data from the 1999 PNAD national household survey (fielded in September 1999) show
that the national poverty rate has risen by 1.3 percentage points compared to 1998. The one-time
poverty gains of stabilization achieved from 1993-95, however, remain essentially intact.
Interestingly, the largest increase in poverty between 1998 and 1999 occurred in the relatively
wealthier southern and metropolitan areas, whereas the increase in poverty in the poor northeast
and rural areas was minimal.
6      Poverty data here come from a Bank Draft Report No. 20475-BR "Attacking Brazil's Poverty" based on
the 1998 PNAD household survey and a food-only poverty line.
7     Data from the Monthly Employmnent Survey, PME, undertaken by IBGE. This survey most likely
exaggerates the negative impact of the crisis since it is limited to six metropolitan areas (most likely the hardest hit
areas) and excludes income from transfer programs which would have compensated for some of the loss of labor
income.



5
9.    Government support for the poor during the difficult 1998-99 period has been overall
effective. Supported by the Bank's Social Protection S/SECAL and a parallel operation by the
IDB, the Federal Government has effectively protected the expenditures for a core set of 22
selected priority social programs in the areas of education, health, labor, and social assistance,
considered to be particularly effective in reaching the poor. Despite the drastic overall cuts in the
1999 budget, allocations for these programs (totaling about R$12 billion) were protected near or
above their 1998 levels. Total actual spending for these protected programns in 1999 has slightly
exceeded the Government commitments. The Government's budget for 2000 implied an increase
in funding for the total of the 22 programs of 20% over the 1999 budget proposal and 12% over
the approved 1999 budget, including very significant increases for relatively new and promising
programs that link transfer payments to poor families to children's school attendance (Child
Labor Eradication Program, and Bolsa Escola).8 Sub-national Governments have also
contributed to social protection in the crisis period, for example through work-fare programs
implemented by several states and municipalities. At the same time, the Federal Government is
proceeding with a program to improve the quality and targeting of spending in the social area.
The remaining third tranche of the IDB's Social Protection Loan is linked to physical
performance indicators of the protected programs and a program of studies and steps to improve
targeting and quality of these programs. In addition, the Bank would follow with particular
interest the strengthening of the implementation framework (monitoring and evaluation,
technical assistance, training, and capacity building for participating municipalities, etc.) for the
transfer programs linked to school attendance discussed above.
10.   An irnportant new development on national social policies and social spending has been
the announcement of the Projeto Alvorada by the President in mid-2000. This program
encompasses spending of about R$11.5bn during 2000-2002 for 14 social programs. Out of this
R$11.5bn, R$4.lbn were already included in the original budget plan/PPA, R$2.2bn are new
regular budget funds, R$1.1bn are proposed World Bank and IDB loans including their state
counterparts, and R$4.2bn are from a new poverty reduction fund that has been approved by
Congress in December 2000. The largest incremental funding from this initiative goes to income
transfer programs linked to school attendance ("Bolsa Escola") and basic sanitation in poor
communities. In both cases, the Bank is providing technical support to the implementing
agencies. The program is directed at the states and microregions with the lowest Human
Development Index. The earlier program name IDH-14 indicated its targeting at the 14 states
with the lowest Human Development Index. Consistent with the emphasis of the Bank's Country
Assistance Strategy, the program is highly concentrated in the poor Northeast region. It is guided
by the principle of better poverty targeting of social spending at both the geographic and the
household level and the improved coordination and integration of social programs across sectors.
As such, the program potentially addresses two of the main shortcomings of social policies in
Brazil to date.
11.   The 2001 budget proposal also includes continued funding for the 22 social protection
programs whose budget was protected, with Bank and IDB support, during the crisis of 1998/99.
These 22 programs were selected at the time as those most essential as social protection during
the crisis. While the Projeto Alvorada includes some of these 22 programs, it has a stronger
medium-term orientation toward social investments addressing the factors underlying poverty
8     The Bank has assessed these programs in a draft Bank report "An Assessment of the Bolsa Escola Program
in Brazil" and an ongoing study of the Child Labor Eradication Program.



6
and low human development. It thus includes increased funding for several areas that were
significantly cut during the crisis, for example sanitation.

13.   The proposed operation contributes to reduce poverty in three ways:



7
* First the macroeconomic management, fiscal management, and debt management
components reduce poverty through their positive impact on growth. Increased growth results
from lower real interest rates. Lower interest rates result from a lower risk premium due to
macroeconomic stability and to lower public sector debt levels. Lower debt levels, in turn,
are to be a consequence of the higher surpluses that the public sector will generate as a result
of better fiscal and debt management. The impact of interest rates on growth has been found
to be significant and powerful.
*  Second, in the expenditure management component, the reform of the public expenditure
process introduced through a new approach to planning (the new PPA) will reduce poverty
through its positive impact on the allocation of public expenditures and on expenditure
management. In the PPA process, program managers monitor the attainment of program
targets and the problems that emerge throughout program implementation. Managers or
higher management at the ministerial level work continuously to resolve those problems.
Moreover programs and program management will be evaluated on a yearly basis. These
evaluations translate into budget programming each year and into changes in the way the
programs are managed. As many public sector programs are targeted to raising the rate of
growth or directly to the poor (see Box 1), particularly in the areas of health and education,
improved budget allocation towards effective programs and management improvement result
in more effective actions to reduce poverty through public expenditure.
- Third, the PPA will also help reduce poverty through four specific programs to improve the
quality of service delivery: i) a public service quality Program through better management
techniques and improved recognition to good civil service teams; (ii) a program to reduce
bureaucratic weight; (iii) a program to improve the performnance of public agencies through
results oriented management; (iv) and a program to improve civil service capacity for results
oriented public management.' Again, since much of public expenditure is targeted to the
poor, these programs should contribute to reduce poverty.
THE FISCAL REFORM PROGRAM
14.   Following the Fiscal and Administrative Reform Loan approved last March, the operation
proposed here is being designed to support a deepening of Brazil's fiscal reforms. Brazirs present
fiscal reform efforts can be classified in four closely related areas: (i) fiscal balance; (ii)
structure, assignment and earmarking of taxes; (iii) allocation and technical efficiency of
expenditures; and (iv) debt management. Reforms in these areas are aimed at improving
economic efficiency through macroeconomic stability, restructuring of taxes and expenditures,
and better choices of risks and return in assets and liabilities. Main issues and reforms in these
areas are discussed in turn, followed by a brief reference to other reforms that have been
completed.
9 See IMF, Brazil - Selected Issues and Statistical Appendix, SM/00/25 1, November 10, 2000, Section VII.
10 Para. 49 provides more detail on these programs.



8
FISCAL SUSTAINABILITY
15.    The Fiscal Stability  Program. Since  1998, as indicated  before, Brazil has been
successfully implementing an IMF supported program to generate primary surpluses that are
consistent with a sustainable public sector net debt path. The Fiscal Stability Program for 1999-
01, introduced by the Federal Government in October 1998 and strengthened in March 1999,
responded to the considerable financial turbulence that Brazil experienced in 1998, as a result of
the adverse international financial environment and Brazil's expansionary fiscal policy through
1998. The program originally called for public sector primary surpluses of 3.1%, 3.25%, and
3.35% of GDP respectively for 1999, 2000, and 2001. Table 1 shows fiscal performance since
1991. The target for 1999 was met (3.13%) and the target for 2000 (revised to 3.4% of GDP due
to revised GDP) is likely to be met as well (the primary surplus through October was 4% of
GDP). The target for 2001 has been revised down to 3.0%  and the target for 2002 was set at
2.7%. By October 2000, the Government had reduced the debt/GDP ratio to 49.0% of GDP
(from 53.4%  in January 1999, its highest point after the floating of the exchange rate) of GDP.
The debt/GDP ratio by the end of 2000 is likely to be within 49%-50%. The original target for
end-2001 under the program (46.5% of GDP) was dropped in October, 2000 due to an
unexpected drop in the growth of the GDP deflator, as measured by IBGE. The Government now
expects that the Debt/GDP ratio will fall within 46%-48%.' To strengthen fiscal sustainability,
the Government is also implementing a major improvement in debt management institutions, as
discussed fuirther below in this report. A declining debt path is a necessary condition to lower
real interest rates, increase growth, and thereby contribute to Brazil's poverty reduction efforts.

17.    The LDO assumes declining real interest rates, from about 26% in 1999 to about 11% in
2003. Given inflation assumptions, the real interest rate would remain stable at around 8% during
2000-03. This scenario reflects a prudent monetary policy that needs to weigh the impact of
declining rates on aggregate demand, on the exchange rate, and on the cost of the public debt.
Under the projections the public sector's interest bill, declines from 7.2% of GDP in 1999 to
4.5% in 2003, as a result of the assumed decline in the interest rate and the debt/GDP ratio.
18.    The LDO assumes GDP growth rates of 4.5%, 5%, and 5%  respectively during 2001-
2003, up from 0.2% and 1.1% respectively during 1998-99. As indicated before, growth has
accelerated since the last quarter of 1999. While the indicated assumptions are higher than
Brazil's recent historical record, they are well below the more bullish assessments of Brazil's
growth potential. The Government has a well developed growth policy framework, that focuses

framework, and on stronger linkages to the world economy. A pessimistic growth rate
throughout, say 3% flat during 2000-2003, would allow an stabilization, but not a decline, of the
debt GDP ratio at around 49%.
19.   Finally, the LDO assumes continued privatization revenues through 2001, more than
offset by new recognition of old liabilities. The Government's policy has been to gradually
recognize past or emerging contingent liabilities. Among these, the major new liability is a court
awarded index correction on accumulated debts that emerged from worker contributions to the
Time in Service Guarantee Fund (Fundo de Garantia por Tempo de Servi,co, FGTS)14. The LDO
anticipates recognition of liabilities amounting to R$13.2 billion in 2001.
20.   Continued implementation of the Government's Fiscal Stability Program for 1999-2001,
and the LDO targets beyond 2001 provide the overall fiscal framework for the proposed
Programmatic Sector Adjustment Loan. The 1999-2001 Program is supported by IMF under the
Stand-by Agreement that expires in December], 2001. The program has been rigorously
implemented. Bank staff will continue closely monitoring macroeconomic policies, including
fiscal policies and management, together with the authorities and in coordination with the IMF,
with a view to early identification of possible areas of concern and corrective actions as
appropriate, so as to help ensure effective implementation of the program supported by the loan.
21.   Fiscal Responsibility. The Fiscal Stability Program  discussed above introduced a
consistent framework for fiscal sustainability. The key issue with aggregate fiscal planning in
Brazil has been the weakness of the link between expenditure and revenue flows, on the one
hand, and net public debt levels, on the other. That is, expenditure levels more often than not
were set without adequate consideration of the implications of those levels on debt, the burden of
which falls on subsequent administrations. By way of example, Table 1 above shows how,
through 1998, sub-national governments ran primary deficits which were not sufficiently offset
by federal primary surpluses. As a result, the burden of interest costs increased the stock of debt.
The example suggests weak constraints on indebtedness, particularly at the state level.
22.   In May of this year, Congress approved a new fiscal code (Complementary Law 101,
2000 - known as Law of Fiscal Responsibility (Lei de Responsabilidade Fiscal), or LFR. This
Law firmly establishes fiscal sustainability planning and implementation to governments at all
levels, as introduced by the Federal Government through its Fiscal Stability Program, described
above. The new law, based on Article 163 of Brazil's Constitution (requiring the regulation of
public finances)1 , covers (i) fiscal targets; (ii) mechanisms to correct deviations from fiscal
targets; (iii) a mechanism to encourage accurate revenue projections; (iv) limits on permanent
expenditure increases, personnel expenditures, net debt, gross bonded federal debt, and
borrowing; (v) prohibition of intergovernmental lending, except through guarantees or by public
14 The FGTS is a mandatory severance pay arrangement funded by an 8% employer contribution on wages that
yields a real annual rate of return of 3% per year. The new liability emerged from a court ruling, following more
than a decade of legal questionings, that awarded creditors of the FGTS a correction of 68.89% in creditor balances
due to under-correction of balances during the failed Bresser (1987) and Collor I and 2 (1989 and 1990)
stabilization plans. The Federal Mortgage Bank (Caixa Economica Federal) has estimated that additional expenses
due to the ruling will amount to R$58 billion.
'5 The LFR covers public finance and debt. It does not regulate the budget process, which is covered by Article 165
(9, 11) of the Constitution. The budget process is presently regulated by Law 4320 of 1964.



11
financial institutions; (vi) transparency (disclosure of fiscal laws, accounts and reports); and (vii)
federal consolidation of overall public sector accounts and a federal registry of public sector
debt. Enactment of the Law represents a major breakthrough in terms of addressing Brazil's
structural fiscal problems: it will allow Brazil to complete the transition from a past where a
disregard for the impact of expenditures on debt led to an unsustainable fiscal stance, to a new
environment in which sustainability comes to the forefront in determining aggregate
expenditures and indebtedness.
23.   Under the LFR, a government (national or sub-national) must establish fiscal targets
(revenues, expenditures, nominal and primary balances, and debt) for the next three years in its
LDO (para. I of LFR Article 4), a key part of the budget cycle (Box 2 describes the budget
cycle). As indicated above (para. 16), the Federal Government established primary surpluses of
2.6%, 2.2%, and 1.8% of GDP for 2001-03 in the 2001 LDO, which also includes targets for the
other fiscal variables required. Under the LFR, 2002 LDOs for all governments are required to
establish and disclose targets. This will provide the federal government with the opportunity,
each year, to assess the overall public sector's fiscal stance for the following three years. Under
the proposed program of Bank support, the first of these assessments would be completed prior
to follow-up loan(s). Under the

26.   The LFR establishes ceilings on the ratio of personnel expenditures to net revenues
(50% for the federal government; 60% for states and municipalities) and on the distribution of
those ceilings among branches of government16. These ceilings are to be met by the end of 2001.
States have been gradually adjusting to these levels. The overall ratio of personnel expenditures
declined from 68.0% in 1998 to 65.6% in 1999.This is close to the adjustment projectedfor 1999
under the Fiscal and Administrative Reform Loan (65.2%o). The projections for 2000 and 2001
respectively are 60.5% and 58.1%. These are likely to be achieved, given stronger revenue
growth during 2000-01.
27.   The LFR provides for numerical debt ceilings set by the Senate. The Government has
submitted to the Senate two draft senate resolutions on the maximum ratio of net debt to net
current revenues for the federal government and for sub-national governments. The draft
resolution on net federal debt establishes a maximum net debt/revenue ratio of 3.5. The ratio is
presently 3.45. The draft resolution of sub-national debt establishes a maximum debt/revenue
ratio of 2.0 for states and of 1.2 for municipalities. As many states and municipalities exceed
these ratios, a linear adjustment during a fifteen year period is contemplated. Most states and
some municipalities will remain subject to debt/revenue conditions under the debt refinancing
agreements that they recently signed with the federal government. Senate approval of these
resolutions is expected prior to Board Presentation of the second or third loan under the
proposed PFRSAL.
28.   In addition, the Government, as required by the LFR, has submitted for Congress
approval a draft bill establishing a maximum ratio of gross bonded federal debt to net current
revenue. The proposed limit of 6.5 exceeds the present ratio (5.5) to account for an expected
issuance of Treasury Bills to be held by the Central Bank, to accommodate new liabilities
emerging from pending debt recognition, and to accommodate the substitution of new bonded
debt for other debt (e.g., contractual debt). Together with the LFR prohibition of
intergovernmental lending, these resolutions are aimed at further strengthening fiscal discipline
through borrowing constraints.
29.   To achieve transparency, the LFR requires governments to disclose and widely
disseminate multi-annual plans (PPA's, see Box 1), LDOs (see Box 1), budgets, public accounts,
public debt, budget implementation reports (issued every two months), and fiscal management
reports (reporting on compliance with LFR provisions and issued every fourth month). Under the
proposed program of Bank support, approval of a second or third loan would follow a dramatic
improvement of the level of information on public finance, particularly with regard to the sub-
national levels. Furthermore, the Government will have achieved the first consolidations of
public accounts (for the year 2000) as well as disclosure of sub-national debt levels in a new
debt registry to be created at the Ministry of Finance.
30.   Enforcement of LFR provisions relies on fiscal and penal remedies. LFR remedies
include prohibition of new credit operations and guarantees and voluntary transfers for several
16 The distributed percentages for the federal legislative, judiciary, executive, and ministerio publico (Attorney
General's Office) respectively are 2.5%, 6%, 40.9%, and 0.6% for the federal level; and 3%, 6%, 49%, and 2% for
the state level. In municipalities, the personnel revenue ratio cannot exceed 54% and the other branches together
cannot exceed 6%. A legal challenge to these percentages was recently rejected by the Supreme Court.



13
events of non-compliance. Furthermore, Congress approved a Fiscal Crimes Law, which
includes dismissal (with possible reparation payments), prison, and fines as possible penalties to
those responsible for various fiscal crimes. The new law (Law No. 10.028 of October 19, 2000),
added non-compliance with certain LFR provisions to Brazil's Penal Code (Decree-Law 2848 of
1940), to Law 1079 of 1950 (covering misconduct by federal and state authorities, including the
president, the attorney general (procurador geral), ministers, governors, and state secretaries),
and to Decree-Law 201 of 1967 (covering misconduct of municipal elected officials). As a result,
under the Penal Code officials that borrow illegally, that include uncommitted expenditures or
expenditures exceeding limits in accounts payable (restos a pagar ), that instruct payment of
non-authorized expenditures, that grant guarantees without required counter-guarantees, that fail
to clear amounts exceeding legal limits in accounts payable, that raise personnel expenditures
during the last year in office of an administration, or that issue unauthorized public debt bonds,
are now subject to imprisonment.
31.   Also, under Law 1079, officials (president, ministers, governors, state secretaries, and
attorneys general) that do not instruct the reduction of debt to legal limits, that instruct or
autnorize expenditures not included in the budget, that fail to repay/cancel loans made exceeding
limits, that fail to repay advanced revenue loans (Antecipagao de Receita Orqamentaria) within
the year for which these are authorized, that instruct or authorize illegal intergovernrnental
lending, that borrow against un-accrued future tax receipts, that use the proceeds of bond issues
for a different destination than that for which they were intended, or that extended or received
voluntary transfers in conflict with legal provisions, are now to be fired. Under Decree-Law 201,
the same applies to municipal elected officials.
32.   Finally, under the new Law of Fiscal Crimes, officials that fail to disseminate the Fiscal
Management Report, to send LDO proposals without the Annex of Fiscal Targets, to reduce
expenditure commitments and payments (movimentadaofinanceira) when required by law, or to
implement measures to reduce personnel expenditures to legal limits, are subject to a personal
fine of 30% of their annual salaries.
33.   Under the proposed PFRSAL, approval of a second or third operation  would follow
corroboration of implementation of the fiscal planning (analysis and consolidation of fiscal
annexes by the Federal Government, para. 23) and transparency provisions (consolidation of
fiscal accounts and registry of public sector debts by the Federal Government, para. 29) of the
LFR..
34.   Other Reforms. The Government's reform programs includes other reforms with a
bearing of aggregate fiscal performance. These include chiefly social security reform,
administrative reform, and the refinancing of sub-national debt and are summarized as follows:
* The debt refinancing agreements with most states and some municipalities strengthen the
budget constraints faced by these governments through (I) a collateralization of debt service
obligations (which are guaranteed by federal transfers, grants, and taxes) which is forcing
fiscal adjustment at the sub-national level ; (ii) a commitment to a set of fiscal targets (gross
debt/revenue   ratio,   primary   balance,   personnel   expenditures,  revenues,
privatization/concessions, investment expenditures.



14
* Under the Administrative Reform approved in 1998, governments will be able to dismiss
personnel when their personnel expenditures exceed limits (full regulation of this provision,
still to be completed, is an expected action prior to the second (or third) loan under the
proposed program of Bank support). Furthermore, the public sector will hire through the
private sector labor regime, under which retirement benefits are much lower (1.8 minimum
salaries) than under the public sector regime (13 minimum salaries, on the average). These
reforms will have an impact only in the long term.
* Social security reforms, which have increased contribution time, reduced retirement time,
reduced pension benefits, or increased contributions. These have temporarily stabilized the
social security deficit.
The Administrative Reform and the refinancing agreements were the focus of the Fiscal and
Administrative Reform Loan approved in March, 2000. As a result of those reforms and of the
related reforms discussed in this proposal, the fiscal performance of Brazil has improved, with a
declining debt burden at the federal level and stabilized the debt burden at the sub-national level
(para. 80). The impact of these reforms is expected to continue during the next several years.
STRUCTURE, ASSIGNMENT AND EARMARKING OF TAXES
35.   Brazil's public sector collects about 30% of GDP in taxes. Brazil's complex tax system
includes individual and corporate income taxes, imnport taxes, an industrial products tax, payroll
taxes, taxes on financial and checking account operations, and gross income taxes, all collected
by the Federal Government; a value added tax and motor vehicle taxes collected by states; and
real estate and service taxes. The federal government shares the income and industrial products
taxes with the states and municipalities. The states share the value added tax with municipalities.
Taxes are earmarked in various ways to expenditures, including earmarking of some federal
taxes to social expenditures as well as a minimum percentage of taxes to education.
36.   Three key issues have been raised about this tax system. First, the tax burden varies
widely across economic activities because value added tax rates vary widely and because the
burden of the cascading taxes, on which the tax system relies extensively, varies widely across
economic activities. The excessive number of taxes has responded partly to the need to raise
federal revenues to cover public sector expenditures that amount today to almost 30% of GDP.
Much of this additional revenue effort has had to be undertaken at the federal level because a
good part of other federal revenues are shared with states and municipalities.
37.   Second, assignment of an origin-based value added tax to states is believed to encourage
an inefficient location of economic activity, as states extend tax subsidies to attract industrial
investments. A related problem is that the origin based tax tends to concentrate revenues in the
large producing states and against exporting states. While this is partly corrected by applying a
lower tax rate on interstate trade, the resulting distribution of the valued added tax remains
inequitable across states
38.   Third, earmarking of revenues has introduced considerable rigidity to fiscal policy, as the
earmarking in effect puts a floor on certain expenditures, possibly limiting efficient choices in
fiscal adjustment and in budget programming. Constitutionally mandated earmarking of revenues
occurs chiefly through the federal social security budget, which earmarks about a third of public



15
sector taxes to social expenditures (education, health, retirement and severance benefits). Other
constitutional earmarking mandates include labor taxes (FGTS and Salario-Educa,do),
earmarked to finance severance payments, housing credit, and education; and 15% of
constitutional transfers and of the ICMS, which are earmarked to finance primary education
expenses. These constitutional mandates earmark about half of total public sector taxes to public
expenditures in the social sector. While these earmarking provisions may improve the stability of
financing of some areas of expenditures, they have also proved to be a constraint on budget
management. As a result of this constraint, Brazil established the Fiscal Stabilization Fund (now
the DRU Desvincula,do das Receitas da Uniao ), which "taxes" 20% of the earmarked proceeds
to improve the flexibility of the budget. Nevertheless, remaining earmarking and expenditure
rigidities have continued to make it difficult to contain expenditures when fiscal considerations
require it. As a matter of fact, expenditure rigidities are a constraint on the PPA process,
inasmuch as this process aims to reallocate expenditures to priority areas.
39.   Fourth, sharing of federal revenues has possibly inhibited tax collection effort at the state
and local levels. States received 20% of its revenues from federal constitutional transfers in
1998, but spent 25% on transfers to municipalities. At the state level, transfers in the form of
debt forgiveness have been the main factor discouraging efforts at better collection of their value
added tax. Municipalities received 71% of their revenues as transfers from the federal
government and the states; this may explain weak efforts to collect the taxes assigned to them
(chiefly the services tax and the urban real estate tax).
40.   Brazil has already implemented a number of reforms aimed at addressing the taxation
issues described above. Recent reforms of the personal income tax have helped to rationalize the
personal income tax base, for instance through partial integration of the personal and business
income tax systems. Also, a recent strengthening of intergovernmental lending provisions
through refinancing provisions (1998 Law 9496 and the related debt refinancing agreements,
supported by the Fiscal and Administrative reform Loan) and the Fiscal Responsibility Law, will
improve incentives for tax collection at the sub-national level. A reform to simplify the
complexity of the tax system and improve tax assignment has been extensively discussed in
Congress, although consensus with the executive has not been achieved yet. The most recent tax
proposal submitted by the Government would (i) establish national legislation for the value
added tax (in lieu of independent state-level legislation); (ii) shift the value added tax from an
origin-based tax to a destination based tax over a seven-year transition period; (iii) and replace
the existing cascading federal taxes by non-cascading taxes over a period of three years. This
proposal, which would require an amendment to the Constitution, is unlikely to be approved
before 2003.
41.   While a fundamental piece of the Government's reform agenda, pending tax reform has
not been included among the actions to be supported by the proposed Programmatic Sector
Adjustment operation. The reason is that, despite strong interest on all sides, the reform remains
controversial and the proposal will require two rounds of votes, with qualified majority, in each
house of Congress. Therefore, given the political cycle, with a change in administration in 2003,
there is a significant likelihood that approval of the reform will not take place within the 2000-02
horizon envisaged by the proposed operation. Nevertheless, should appropriate tax reform be
approved, the Bank would consider it and its implementation as part of the reforms to be
supported by a future loan, under the program of Bank support.



16
ALLOCATIONAL AND TECHNICAL EFFICIENCY OF EXPENDITURES
42.   In Brazil's budget process (see Box 2), the PPA is the fundamental document that aims to
establish public sector priorities. A major fiscal reform underway is comprised by the actions to
make the PPA relevant, operational, and effective. The following paragraphs describe the
background of public sector planning and the reforms that have been introduced with the 1996-
99 and 2000-03 PPAs.
43.   Strateaic Budget Planning. Allocational and technical efficiency of expenditures (the
optimal distribution and management of expenditures) depends on adequate budget planning and
implementation environment and systems, including a modern public administration. Brazil's
modernized public administration has its origin in the 1930s, when the Getulio Vargas regime
conferred a new development role on the state and systems for a meritocratic civil service, public
procurement, and budget control were established. 17 The first systematic national plan  (the
1956-61 Targets Plan or Plano de Metas), implemented by the govermnent of Juscelino
Kubitscheck, identified "thirty targets/goals for specific bottlenecks in the economy" and their
achievements "required the cooperation of different government agencies as well as different
private sector actors."'8 The Targets Plan is usually described as having been a relative success
as a means to promote import-substitution industrialization in Brazil.
44.   Successive governments continued relying on various national development plans to
stimulate the country's industrial development. However, by the early 1980s, fiscal crises,
institutional disarray within the Government, the "exhaustion" of the import-substitution model
of economic development, and other structural constraints (e.g. the oil shocks) undermined
planning. During much of the 1980s and until the mid- 1 990s, high inflation resulting from fiscal
crises made matters worse, as budget execution inevitably diverged significantly from approved
budgets, rendering multi-year plans basically unusable as a tool for economic management. Thus
the capacity and the tradition of planning that the Brazilian state had acquired by the 1 950s (in
spite of the growing ineffectiveness of the more recent plans) were lost to a large extent in the
1980s.
45.   Three  structural developments  changed  the  planning  environment.  First, as
decentralization gained ground, particularly with the 1988 constitutional reform, planning
became more complex, as it also became decentralized and thus more subject to local approaches
and to political bargaining between the states and the federal government. Second, trade
liberalization and privatization during the 1 990s changed the focus of planning from industrial
promotion through protection and direct production of goods and services to regulation,
expenditures in social services, and certain strategic infrastructure investments where private
investors are unlikely to come in. Third, stabilization and improved fiscal discipline during the
second half of the 1990s now allows for an improved link of plans to budget.
46.   The Multiannual Plan. Against this background, the Government began development of
a new PPA framework with the previous PPA (1996-1999; Brasil en Afao), a program that
identified a number of key regional investments within a new planning framework (Box 3). The
distinguishing characteristics of the new PPA approach, as implemented thus far, are as follows:
1 7 Annex I provides a brief historical account of the development of the federal administration in Brazil.
1 Barbara Geddes, Politician's Dilemma: Building State Capacity in Latin America, (University of California Press,
1994, p. 67).



17
* The new PPA, entitled AvanVa Brasil, is based partially on an earlier diagnostic study
(Estudo dos Eixos Nacionais de Integraqao e Desenvolvimento, or Study of National Axes
for Integration and Development) that, based on the geographic and economic characteristics
of each of Brazil's region, identified priority investments of R$317 billion to be undertaken
by the public and private sector over eight years.
* The PPA is also based on other fiscal reforms, including setting a multi-year spending
framework, and introducing program and performance budgeting. Specifically, in Avanqa
Brasil, all Federal Government expenditures are grouped in 388 Programs, each with a
performance indicator and clear 2003 target (e.g., infant mortality levels). Each program is
comprised of set of activities or actions deemed relevant for achieving the target. A program
manager was appointed for each of the 388 programs, and is responsible for coordinating the
actions to achieve the PPA objective. The 2001 Budget was submitted on a program basis. In
budget formulation, resources (calculated by the Planning Ministry, not by the line
ministries) are allocated by program and objectives, rather than to administrative units
(Ministries). Avanqa Brasil also integrates capital and current expenditures under the same
Program categories, thus reinforcing the objective-based programmatic approach to public
expenditure management.

* Appointment of a manager for each project
* Flow of funds in proportion to progress of each project
* Management Information System: linkage of managers and other agents in a computerized network
* Cooperation to eliminate restrictions
Management and strategic content are the essence of the Brazil in Action program.
For each project, clear objectives and goals to be reached, physical and financial planning, cost controls, timeframes
and quality were defined. A manager responsible for implementing the goals was appointed.
The manager is charged with the task of organizing actions in various areas, mobilizing resources, maintaining
partnerships and overcoming obstacles to achieving the objectives.
A management information system was created. This is a computerized network operating in real time, linking all the
managers and partners and affording immediate access to data on all the projects. It became easier to identify
restrictions on the performance of each project, facilitating the search for solutions on the part of the government and
its partners. The system also made it possible to base the release of funds on the progress of each project. "

August 3, 2000. By Annex 1, Page 3.
* The PPA also has strategic aspects. Of the 388 programs, 87 have been designated as
strategic programs of the Government, meaning these program objectives are top
Government priorities with a preference in budget execution over other discretionary
programs. Further, the PPA explicitly acknowledges the means of program implementation,
or ways the goals would be attained. Specifically, the federal PPA is more aware and explicit
of the partnerships with sub-national governments and with the private sector in the
implementation of PPA goals. For example, in the delivery of health outcomes, a strong
interface between federal transfers and sub-national implementation is critical for success. In
infrastructure investment with a public interest, strong private sector ownership, financing,
and/or management are also necessary for attaining the goals. In this regard, the Government
is establishing the Brazilian Network of Investment Promotion (Invest in Brazil), which will

disseminate investment opportunities with the expectation of attracting investors to the

* A manager for each program has been appointed, with a view to promote better results
through management and accountability. In terms of management, appointed managers are
presently responsible for monitoring programs to identify problems in implementation and
for promoting coordination among relevant actors to exploit potential synergy from related
activities. In terms of accountability, there is a program performance monitoring system in
place (the SIG) and outcomes are reported to the Ministry of Planning, where a team of 40
professionals is using an evaluation framework developed by the Govemnnent to assess each
of the 388 PPA programs and report to the President by the end of March 2001. The
assessment is to be used to adjust PPA implementation and determine proposed priorities
under the next draft LDO to be submitted to Congress on April 30 2001. The Government
also plans to review its general program evaluation framework during 2001 with Bank
cooperation under the possible technical assistance loan. To further promote transparency
and accountability, the SIG is available on the internet, with the name and phone number of
each manager included, in addition to budget execution and performance data.

47.    In sum, the chief accomplishments of Avan a Brasil thus far include:
* a valuable diagnosis of development needs (the Eixos study) discussed above;

* introduction of a management arrangement to encourage coordination and an MIS to assist in

48.   The Government is now aiming to consolidate the new PPA approach. The two key
strategic challenges for 2000-01 are:
A.    Effectively inte2rate plannin2 and budaetin2 through: better coordination between the
planning and budget offices in each ministry, and between the Ministry of Planning and Ministry
of Finance (Treasury) on annual spending allocations; full-cost allocation to programs to allow
better resource allocation decisions; improved linkages between the financial management
system (SIAFI) and planning (SIG); and
B.    Improve technical efficiency in the delivery of programs through better arrangements
for managerial and ministerial action; these arrangements could include closer involvement of
line ministries in determining targets and costs and should achieve a closer match between the
authority and accountability of program managers.
Achieving both of these strategic objectives will require actions to improve evaluation, through
the following actions:
* assess progress in the prescribed targets (first round of program monitoring): assessment
of progress is part of the general monitoring/evaluation activities underway covering all
programs;
* use of the first evaluation results making decisions for the 2002 LDO and budget. The
evaluation is presently being completed. The evaluation report will be delivered to Congress
together with the draft LDO on April 30, 2001;



20
*  introduce deeper program  evaluations of selected programs, with involvement at the
ministerial level and including (i) a model of how government actions influence the outcome
of interest (i.e., disentangling the effect of government actions from serial trends in the
performance indicators) and (ii) better estimates of the full costs required to achieve the
outcomes. These evaluations are likely to begin in 2002.
*  Further develop the general monitoring and evaluation system, including a system  to
protect priority programs, particularly when overall resources decline, to be linked into the
planning and budgeting cycle. This development, which the Government expects to develop
during 2001, is an area that could form part of the technical assistance project currently under
preparation.
49.    A  central government's objective is to improve public sector service delivery to
Brazilian citizens. At the broadest, macro level, fiscal planning through the LFR is expected to
continue delivering the improved macroeconomic stability that Brazilians have been enjoying
during the last five years. At the allocational level, the PPA goals are expected to better match
Brazilian citizens' expectations of the kinds of services to be provided by the public sector. And
at the technical efficiency level, PPA administration seeks to maximize the "bang for the buck"
in terms of the goals that have been set to be achieved. The Government's concern with
delivering services to its citizens is manifest in four mutually related additional PPA programs:
(i) to increase service quality [the Quality Program (Qualidade no Servi,o Publico), one of the
388 PPA programs) through better management techniques and improved recognition to good
civil service teams'9; (ii) to reduce bureaucratic weight through a new program to simplify
administrative procedures (Programa Nacional de Desburocratiza,do) that has already produced
420 simplification measures, with 248 of those with results on the ground (i.e., facilitating the
citizen's dealings with the government; (iii) to improve the performance of public agencies
through a results oriented Public Management Program (Programa Gestdo Public
Empreendedora); (iv) to improve civil service capacity for results oriented public management
(Programa Valorizafao do Servidor PNblico). In addition, service delivery is being encouraged
by dismissal of public servants that have engaged in poor behaviors (corruption, poor treatment
of the public, disruption at work), with federal dismissals increasing from 307 in 1996 to 454 in
1999) and by the Rede Governo, a web page (1500 hits per day at present) providing information
links to the public on public services and public service providers20.
50.   Delivering better services to citizens means, in particular, better public services to the
poor. A major part of public expenditures are allocated to health, education and other social
services, which largely benefit the poor. Therefore the PPA, both as a process that improves the
use of resources and as specific programs that benefit the poor may become a key lever for
public action to reduce poverty.
51.    The reforms of the PPA process are a key component of the set of reforms that would be
supported by the proposed PFRSAL and follow up loan(s). The first loan would support the
19 The Government established, five years ago, the Helio Beltrao Prize, which recognizes excellence. In 2000, 16
teams out of 75 candidate teams, will be recognized.

actions that have already been undertaken (summarized in para. 47). The next loan(s) would
