Government's monitoring and evaluation system and the integration of planning and evaluation
to the budget through this system and through the LDO.
52.   Reduction of Personnel Expenditures. Another policy to improve the allocation of
public expenditures has been to encourage a reduction in the burden of personnel expenditures,
including ceilings on the share of those expenditures in total revenues. These expenditures are
comprised by wages to the active civil service as well as by benefits to retirees. In several cases,
personnel expenditures have exceeded 80% of net revenues, with a few cases where the revenues
did not suffice to cover personnel costs. Excessive government expenditures has responded
partly to excessive wages in some parts of the civil service, excessive employment in many
areas, and a generous retirement benefit regime.
53.   The Fiscal and Administrative Reform Loan approved last March supported the actions to
allow governments to reduce personnel expenditures.21 These actions included passage of the
19th Amendment to the Constitution, which removed mandatory tenure for the civil service and
established conditions under which governments would be allowed to implement redundancy
programs as well as the process to implement those programs. Furthermore, the Fiscal
Responsibility Law includes a related provision that sets ceilings to the share of personnel
expenditures in net revenues and to the distribution of those shares among the legislative,
judiciary, and executive branches of an individual government (para. 26). Ceilings on the total
shares of personnel expenditures, which had been enacted in previous laws, and a stronger
budget constraint led sub-national governments to reduce the share of personnel expenditures
chiefly by containing the growth of personnel expenditures while revenues increase (para. 26).
The ceilings provide a necessary constraint where there are strong pressures to raise employment
and wages. Pending legislation (the Inadequate Performance Dismissal Bill) that will determine
conditions for dismissal of government employees by reason of poor performance, as well as the
set of careers in the civil service that are subject to the redundancy provisions will provide
governments with an additional tool of adjustment under conditions of excessive personnel
expenditures (i.e., when the share of those expenditures exceeds the limits established in the
LFR. The second or third loan of the proposed program of Bank support would support approval
of this pending legislation.
54.   Systems for Monitoring Expenditures. Brazil's Integrated Financial Management
Systems (IFMS). A fundamental requirement to achieve fiscal efficiency is that the budget
reflect strategic priorities and expenditures be made and tracked accordingly. Asset and liability
management is also an important component for the achievement of fiscal efficiency. Effective
monitoring and evaluation of expenditures, assets and liabilities, are key components of efficient
fiscal policies and operations. The planning and management of government programs on a
multiyear basis, such as intended by the Brazilian PPA, further supports the achievement of
targeted budgetary outputs and outcomes. IFMS facilitates the administration of the relevant
information flows, tracking revenues and expenses, and providing managers with comprehensive
information on the financial and physical execution of the budget required for planning, decision
making and evaluation.
21 Two prior Bank operations have supported social security reforms to contain the impact of the generous
retirement benefits of the civil service, which are constitutionally protected.



22
55.   In 1987, Brazil's federal government established an IFMS, known as SIAFI. The system
was placed under the responsibility of the Natural Treasury Secretariat (STN) at the Ministry of
Finance (MOF). Through an extensive telecommunications network, SIAFI outreaches to
approximately 4,650 spending units under the federal government's and the social security
budgets. It is also used for the consolidation of the financial statements of the federal, state and
municipal governments, to produce the consolidated financial statements of the Union. Through
SIAFI, the SNT performs the financial planning of the federal budget; centrally processes and
records all budgetary and financial transactions of spending units, thus effectively controlling
the execution of the budget and automatically making the accounting postings thereon,
producing the federal government's financial statements and financial reports required at the
various government management levels; and, concentrates all federal government cash revenues
in the treasury's unified account (Conta Unica do Tesouro) held at the Central Bank, and
centralizes payments made on behalf of spending units during budget execution. SIAFI offers
government managers consultation and reporting facilities, providing access to current and past
fiscal year data bases, and permits the importation of data to other management applications.
56.   While SIAFI remains a sound operating IT system, it is in urgent need of technological
upgrading after 13 years in operation.  It was originally developed using programming
methodologies and languages that have been largely surpassed and its internal architecture
permits user access only by way of terminals, disallowing the use of Windows and other graphic
environment facilities. Its data base technology has also been overcome not having the
flexibility and agility offered by a new generation of graphic environment data base versions for
the expedient customization SIAFI's functionality pursuant changes in financial management
legislation, policies and processes. This is particularly important in terms of new functional
requirements related to the PPA and the LRF.
57.   To address these and other relevant technological and functional issues, the government
has designed the SIAFI 21st Century project, aiming at replacing the current SIAFI with a new
system using the latest technological advances, improving its current budget, cash management
and accounting capabilities, and accommodating its functionality to the new fiscal management
framework defined by the PPA, the LRF and emphasis on asset/liability management. The
project calls for important enhancements in the system's architecture, establishing a three-layer
structure (data/ applications/user interface) which will greatly facilitate the future adaptation of
the system to technological and functional changes, and that will offer user-friendly
environments and data retrieval and analysis tools so that the systems becomes a real managerial
system, rather than remain mainly an operations and financial recording facility.
58.   The new system will be structured in four basic subsystems. The budget, financial and
patrimonial subsystem would perform most budgetary and cash management functions of the
current SIAFI, with enhanced financial planning capabilities and with added functionality
serving the information requirements of the PPA and the LRF. A second subsystem will focus
on asset/liability administration, with capability to handle the information flows and the
management requirements of an assets portfolio amounting to approximately US$185 billion,
and a liabilities portfolio, including internal and external debt obligations, amounting to
approximately US$335 billion.  A new and third subsystem  will serve the monitoring
requirements established by the LRF for the federal and particularly for the state and municipal
governments, including the management of constitutional and voluntary transfers made by the
federal level to local governments. A fourth subsystem is intended to modernize the internal



23
management of the STN, implementing work flow and other technological systems to improve
operational efficiency and controls.
59.   While some project preparation progress was made during 2000, the program will be
largely developed between 2001 and 2002. Development of the budget, financial and patrimonial
subsystem is likely to be completed in the 2nd quarter of 2001, with completion of the other
modules expected by the 2nd quarter of 2002. It is planned to be fully operational at the
beginning of 2003. However, a number of modules required for the preparation and planning of
the 2003 budget are scheduled to come.
60.   With these improvements the Government will be better equipped to a key requirement
of good public sector performance: output and outcomes oriented execution of the budget; close
monitoring of revenues and expenditures; efficient asset/liability management; and, strong fiscal
management evaluation capabilities. This approach is consistent with the new PPA/LFR
framework, which call for improved integration between planning, budgeting and spending.
61.   As indicated, the Government has begun to implement parts of this reform. The second
or third operation under the proposed program, would support (i) completed improvements in
the budget and patrimonial sub-system, with completion measured as effective use by
government spending units, and (ii) progress towards complethig the other module within the
timetable referred to in para. 59.
EFFICIENCY OF DEBT MANAGEMENT
62.   Debt management is a fundamental determinant of macroeconomic vulnerability and of
fiscal performance. Interest payments on the public debt turned Brazil's primary surplus of 4.3%
of GDP into a nominal deficit of 3.4% of GDP, thereby accounting for 7.7% of GDP from
January to September of 2000. While a primary surplus can offset the burden of interest
payments to generate a sustainable debt path, efficient debt management can reduce the primary
effort required through a choice of a debt portfolio structure that mitigates the risk component of
interest rates faced by the public sector. The following paragraphs describe recent debt trends,
debt policies, and debt institutions in Brazil, as well as the recent institutional reforms that are
bringing debt management closer to international practice.
63.   Growth of Government Debt. Brazil's net public sector debt grew very rapidly from
28.5 percent of GDP in 1995 to 53.4 percent in January 1999, raising doubts about the
sustainability of economic policy and on the country's capacity for proper debt management.
These concerns were compounded by the short maturity of the domestic public debt, and a
rapidly changing operating environment characterized by increased reliance on market sources
for financing, greater exposure to financial risks resulting from financial liberalization, and larger
international capital flows. The urgency of the situation was indicated by the international
attention paid to Brazil's debt structure as a source of vulnerability in late 1998, and by the
decision to shift from exchange rate targeting to inflation targeting in the first half of 1999.
Moreover, the period since March 1999 has been characterized by a rapid evolution in debt
policies to take advantage of the new monetary policy environment.
64.   Composition of Domestic Debt. As of September 2000, the net public sector debt of R$
548 billion (or 48.5% of GDP was comprised of federal net debt (29.4% of GDP), sub-national



24
net debt (16.7%, mostly state governments), and the debt of state-owned enterprises (2.4%,
mostly state enterprises). Net internal debt constituted 39.0% of GDP, while net external debt
was 9.5%. Over time, the share of net public sector domestic debt relative to net external debt
has increased. Domestic debt comprised 66 percent of total debt in January 1997, but increased
to 78 percent in December 1998. Despite the devaluation in January 1999, the relative shares, as
of June 2000, have remained at this level. Gross federal securities outstanding in the domestic
market in June 2000, excluding Treasury securities held by the Central Bank, amounted to
R$511.9 billion or 45.3 percent of GDP. The total stock of federal securities has increased
substantially since 1993, reflecting increased financing costs due to higher interest rates,
recognition of debts, and recapitalization of state-owned banks.
65.   Government Debt Policies. Since achieving price stability in 1994, the Treasury
authorities' medium-term objective has been to increase the maturity and duration of the liability
portfolio by increasing the share of fixed-coupon securities22. Nevertheless, the speculative
attack of October 1997 compelled the Central Bank to raise interest rates substantially, thereby
imposing a capital loss on the holders of longer-maturity fixed-rate debt and consequently
reducing demand for these securities. After a brief period of issuing floating rate instruments at
the end of 1997, the Treasury was able to resume the issuance of fixed-rate securities as domestic
interest rates returned to the pre-crisis level in the first quarter of 1998, but the Russian crisis in
August 1998 again provoked an speculative attack, resulting in a return to high interest rates. In
response to these various shocks, the share of fixed-rate instruments declined from 55.6 percent
in May 1998 to 3.5 percent of federal securities in the market in December 1998, while floating
rate instruments increased to 69.1% and dollar-indexed instruments rose to 21% by the same
date. The January 1999 devaluation pushed the latter share to 30.4 percent in January 1999. The
drastic changes in the compositions of the instruments had a substantial effect on the duration of
the portfolio, increasing its exposure interest rate risks. However, the refinancing risk did not
increase in similar proportions because the Treasury extended the average maturity of the
portfolio using long-term floaters ( 3 and 5 years). By September 2000, the debt structure had
improved significantly, with the share placed at fixed rates recovering to 15.7%; the share placed
at floating rates declining to 53.2%; and the share of dollar indexed debt declining to 20.5%,.
66.   On the external front, the long period of debt negotiation with the commercial banks and
the Paris Club concluded in 1993, resulting in the issuance of Brady bonds and opening a new
era of relationship with the commercial sources. Since then the government has actively
increased the share of financing from external sources relative to domestic sources. However, the
government's external debt policy has not been driven so much by its financing needs, but rather
by the need to issue the minimum required to establish a yield curve in international markets and
thereby facilitate the access of private issuers to these markets. Like other Latin borrowers, it has
also been involved in the restructuring of its Brady debt.
67.   Institutional and Leaal Framework The Central Bank was the main institution that
managed the public external debt in Brazil up until the period between 1986 and 1998 when the
transfer of debt management responsibilities from the Central Bank to the Treasury began with
the establishment of the STN within the Treasury. Under the new arrangement, the STN was to
be responsible for the administration of both internal and external debt. The Treasury is
22 The Government's letter of Development Policies (Annex 1) provides a more detailed statement of debt policy
objectives, which are listed in para. 70.



25
therefore legally responsible for making the ultimate decisions regarding the issuance, issue size,
maturity, timing and characteristics of the Treasury securities. Nevertheless, the Central Bank
remained an important issuer of federal securities in the domestic market due to its monetary
policy objectives and continued to influence the administration of the public debt. Furthermore,
the Central Bank continues to manage the government's access to the international capital
market and the dialogue with the lenders involved in the Brady bonds and Paris Club
negotiations because of the complexity and sensitivity of the issues at stake and the existent
capacity in the Central Bank.
68.   This institutional dispersion is further complicated by the involvement of several
government agencies in the management of the multilateral debt. Currently the Treasury has only
a marginal involvement in deciding on the size of the contracts with multilateral banks.
Decisions in this regard are mainly driven by the Ministry of Planning and COFIEX,
independent of a financial assessment of the overall debt management policy (although the
Treasury is represented in COFIEX). At the operational level, each of the Ministries acting as
implementing agencies are responsible for budgeting, accounting and payment of these loans,
thus multiplying the number of back offices involved in the management of this debt. Dispersion
is also reflected in the legal framework, which lacks unification of the various debt provisions
into a comprehensive code. Existing provisions also lack a clear definition of objectives for debt
management, although there are implicit references to some of these objectives.
69.   Reform of Debt Management Institutions. The Government is presently undertaking a
reform of its debt management institutions. In addition to continued actions to transfer the debt
management functions to the Treasury, the latter is upgrading its organizational and professional
capacity to manage the debt. These changes will allow the Government to better address debt
management challenges. These challenges include a desirable move towards a more even
distribution of maturities (e.g., by issuing medium and long term floating rate bonds and/or
replacing discounted bullet bonds by coupon amortization bonds) and increasing the use of
fixed-rate instruments. A good balance in the use of available instruments should allow Brazil to
minimize the volatility of the real interest rate paid on the debt, the parameter which, together
with the primary deficit, directly affects debt dynamics.
70.   The Government has already undertaken the following actions to improve debt
management institutions:
*  decided to centralize debt management at the treasury (through a formal agreement between
the Federal Government and the Central Bank): this aims to strengthen the governance
framework, which should clearly indicate the authority that borrows and issues new debt,
invests, and undertakes transactions on the government's behalf. This governance practice is
an important component of sovereign debt management, given the size of government debt
portfolios.
*  created analytical middle office at the Treasury23: this aims to provides the research,
analysis, decision support, information and planning activities that are necessary and useful
to senior management and to the portfolio decision makers (see Box 6);
23 In debt management parlance, the front office is the point of contact with the markets; the middle office monitors
information, evaluates performance, and formulates policy options; and the back office implements transactions
(e.g., settlement).



26
*  created unified back office within the treasury;
*  created unit to assess and quantify contingent liabilities;
*  introduced a risk management framework (Asset Liability Management), with preliminary

73.   Improved institutions will allow Brazil's federal government to better manage the risks
and returns of its assets and liabilities, as follows: (i) better governance through centralization at
the treasury will strengthen accountability and the focus on risk/cost objectives; (ii) written
guidelines and procedures and a code of conduct will improve overall performance and also
strengthen accountability; (iii) a stronger rniddle office will achieve the needed capacity to
analyze and formulate policy options; (iv) secondary market improvements and stronger back
and front offices will facilitate the implementation of chosen debt policies. The second or third
loan wouldfollow progress in the areas indicated in para. 71. Specifically, key actions regarding
governance (transfer of external debt operations to the Treasury and issuance of the code of
conduct and debt management guidelines and procedures), capacity (issuance of regular middle
office reports on the debt portfolio and STN operations and completion of an IT development
plan), and benchmark (annual borrowing plan, initial benchmark and medium term strategy)
would be completed prior to the second or third loan.
EXPECTED IMPACTS OF THE REFORM PROGRAM
74.   The overall goal of the program is to strengthen the foundations for Brazil's performance
on growth, poverty reduction and public service delivery to citizens. The direct goals of the
government's reform program can be stated as follows:
* improving fiscal sustainability, including the pursuit of a low cost of the public debt within
acceptable risk levels;
* improving the allocational and technical efficiency of expenditures, including an
improvement in the delivery of public services to Brazil's citizens.
All of the four key components discussed above (the LFR, the PPA, the SIAFI 21st century, and
debt management) have a bearing on one or both of these two broad goals. Implementation of the
LFR institutionalizes and deepens fiscal adjustment and provides an aggregate framework for the
allocation of public resources. The PPA provides a stronger programmatic orientation to the
budget which should contribute to aggregate sustainability by improving the efficiency of public
expenditures and the extent to which these expenditures meet the needs of Brazil's citizens. The
reform of the government's integrated financial management system improves budget
programming, budget execution and debt management, all with a bearing on fiscal sustainability
and the allocation of expenditures. And better debt management is aimed at minimizing debt
costs for given risk levels, with an impact on fiscal sustainability. As indicated (para. 12), the
program is expected to contribute to poverty reduction objectives through higher growth and
better direct poverty reduction programs.
75.   The Law  of Fiscal Responsibility. Analytically, the LFR provides a number of
incentives for fiscal prudence. These are:
* A framework for ex-ante constraints: this includes the fiscal targets (on revenues,
expenditures, the primary deficit and debt) that governments must issue with their budget
guidelines laws;



29
*  A framework to plan on fiscal risks: this is provided by the Annex of Fiscal Risks that
governments must issue with their budget guidelines laws.
*  A framework for ex-post reconciliation and corrective action: this is the obligation to report
on fiscal performance, compare it with fiscal targets, and take corrective actions;
*  numerical limits on "permanent" expenditures and on personnel expenditures (the most
important of current expenditures): these are the constraints that personnel expenditure/net
revenue ratios not exceed numerical limits (para. 26) and the constraint that charges
permanent expenditures (legally mandatory expenditures for more than two years) not exceed
charges permanent revenues;
*  A stronger framework for numerical limits or debt: these are constraints on the ratio of debt
to net revenues that are issued by the Senate on debt and constraints on borrowing LFR;
*  increased transparency of fiscal planning and fiscal performance increased transparency is
triggered by the obligation to disseminate PPA and budget laws; governments' reports on
budget execution and budget management; and public accounts.
76.   Compliance with LFR is encouraged by present budget constraints (debt and borrowing),
by LFR provisions prohibiting certain voluntary transfers and borrowing to non-complying
governments, and by penal provisions established by the new Law of Fiscal Crimes.
77.   The impact of the LFR should be analyzed in the context of the other government
policies with a bearing on fiscal sustainability. These reforms include the Government's 1998
Fiscal Stability Plan, which runs through 2001 and contemplates measures to generate fiscal
surpluses that suffice to reduce the overall public sector's debt/revenue ratio; the refinancing
agreements with most states and some municipalities, which strengthened the budget constraints
faced by sub-national governments; and social security and administrative reforms, which are
helping contain personnel expenditures.
78.   The LFR was issued in May 2000. Given the mechanics of the law, its effects began with
the Federal Government's issuance of its 2001 Fiscal Guidelines Law in July 2000, which
included the Fiscal and Risk Annexes mandated by the LFR The federal fiscal annex presents
federal primary surplus targets that decline from 2.6% of GDP in 2001 to 1.8% of GDP in 2003.
The net debt targets decline from 28.0 percent of GDP in 2001 to 25% of GDP in 2003. The
fiscal risks annex indicates R$13 billion of old liabilities to be recognized during 2001. The
federal LDO illustrates the establishment of ex-ante constraints on fiscal behavior, which is
achieved through the LDO legal commitment to meet the targets. Of course, this constraint will
work only if the government that issues the LDO is subject to a strong budget constraint, which
the LFR also contributes to put in place through ceilings on debt and constraints on borrowing
and which was strengthened by the debt-refinancing agreements with most states and some
municipalities by guaranteeing debt service obligations with intergovernmental transfers.



30
79.   LFR effects are continuing with reporting obligations. Reports, including bimonthly
summary budget execution reports and fiscal management reports (issued every four months),
are beginning to be issued, some appearing in government websites. By April of 2001,
Governments must issue, disclose and disseminate their 2000 fiscal accounts, and the federal
government must consolidate those accounts by mid-year. By April of 2001, governments
(except municipal governments with population less than 50,000), must issue LDOs with 2002
fiscal target annexes and fiscal risk annexes.

operation after approval in May 1999. For example, in the Annex of Fiscal Targets to the LDO
for the 2001 budget, the federal government aims at reducing the ratio of federal debt to GDP to
28.0%, 26.3%, and 25.1 respectively in 2001-03, from 30.1% in October 2000. It federal
revenues rise at least at the same rate as GDP, federal debt/revenue ratios will also decline.
Furthermore, most sub-national debt is covered by refinancing agreements which have declining
debt/revenue targets. These should be reinforced by the LFR as it enters into full operation.
27 Debt ceilings under the LFR are to be expressed as ratios to net revenues rather than as ratios to GDP (Para. 3 of
LFR Article 30).



31
81.   The Multi-Annual Plan. By meeting the challenges indicated in para. 48., Brazil's PPA
process will be part of a medium term expenditure framework (MTEF) that will have a positive
effect on all three outcomes that are expected to result from such a framework: aggregate fiscal
discipline, allocation of resources in accordance with strategic priorities, and technical
efficiency. The MTEF in Brazil is comprised by the LFR, which furnishes the overall resource
envelope, and by the budget cycle (PPA,LDO, budget Law), where priorities are defined and
implemented. The tools to achieve these goals are program budgeting; performance budgeting;
program evaluation; introduction of program managers and improving program coordination;
serving as a vehicle for strategic allocation of resources; establishing a multi-year planning
horizon, within a multi-year expenditure envelope. While the ultimate effect of these reforms is
highly dependent on the unique institutional and political environment in each country, and
measuring the precise impact is practically impossible given the nature of fiscal reform
programs, based on the general experience in other countries with some components of the PPA
reform, it is likely that decision-making, resource allocations, and technical efficiency of
spending will be positively effected by the reforms.
Improved Aggregate Fiscal performance at all Government levels
82.   Stronger aggregate fiscal discipline will be supported by a consistent and realistic
resource framework (which is now being provided by better fiscal planning under the Fiscal
Responsibility Law), undertaking specific budget commitments only within the available multi-
year resource envelope, and by  defining expenditure priorities (which should assist in
expenditure reductions when budget reductions are necessary). Better aggregate fiscal planning
will also increase the predictability of both policy and funding so that programs can be planned
ahead and be sustained. While there is some potential for PPA to increase demand for public
resources, the over-all impact is likely to be a positive reinforcement of fiscal discipline.
Moreover, if PPA improves the effectiveness and efficiency (intensiveness of use) of public
resources, more can be achieved within current public resource levels, which may serve to lower
demand for additional resources or allow reallocation of resources to higher priorities.
Improved Allocation and Technical Efficiency of Expenditures
83.   The PPA introduces several reforms that provide more objective information on what
public spending purchases and what is achieved, which can improve decision-making and
resource allocation. Allocation of resources in accordance with strategic priorities will be
positively affected by the PPA process itself, which defines strategic programs, their targets, and
the actions to achieve those targets. Technical efficiency, or efficient and effective use of
resources in the implementation of strategic priorities, will be positively affected by introduction
of a managerial system that mobilizes the allocated resources in an effective way, and improved
coordination of programs which cut across sectors but impact the same objective (e.g., good
water for better health outcomes). To achieve the full benefit of the program manager concept
may require a stronger institutional role for program managers in their interaction with the
expenditure executing ministries, and possibly increased administrative flexibility for line
agencies. Other PPA measures jointly improve allocation and technical efficiency:
* performance measures and increases accountability, which allows better resource allocation
as well as pressure for improved efficiency and effectiveness;



32
* program evaluations, which can improve decision-making and resource allocations, as well
as improve effectiveness and efficiency of programs; serves as a vehicle for selecting
strategic programs, and improving resource allocations. An important challenge here is to
introduce an evaluation system that will yield objective information on the effectiveness of
programs, and then incorporate this information into improved program management and
resource allocations.
Over-all, these reforms are likely to have a positive impact on resource allocation and technical
efficiency, in themselves as well as a package of reforms. Nevertheless, it should be
acknowledged that the PPA is not a guarantee of good public expenditure performance. Future
evaluations are likely to uncover programs that perform poorly. The PPA is expected o provide a
framework to uncover these programs and improve their performance. Hence, the proposed loan
supports the PPA fundamentally as a process, and not as an endorsement of all public
expenditure programs.
84.   The Integrated Financial Management System. The ultimate benefits of the SIAFI
21"t Century project are really the benefits of the other key reforms of the Government's reform
program. The SIAFI 21st Century Project aims at adapting the functionality of the existing
SIAFI to the new financial management model of the federal government, which establishes
program-based multi-annual planning (PPA) framework, and calls for more operational
integration between  planning, budgeting, treasury, debt management and asset/liability
management (as required by the implementation of the LFR). This replaces the historical
bureaucratic public administration model with a management model supporting productivity and
results. Therefore, the SIAFI 215t project is an integral component of the Governments fiscal
reform program.
85.   As an integral component of the reform program, SIAFI 21St Century will provide the IT
framework to enable LFR benefits (overall fiscal balance), PPA benefits (chiefly better
allocation and more effective use of public resources), and debt management benefits (better debt
cost/risk outcomes) to materialize. In these regards, the project will incorporate new management
approaches and techniques that are conceptually absent in the current SIAFI, including program-
based multi-annual planning; monitoring and evaluation of the physical and financial execution
of the budget from a results-oriented management perspective rather than only from an
accounting perspective; costing of government outputs and outcomes, enhanced public debt and
asset/liability management; and, improved accountability at the federal and local government
levels.
86.   The benefits indicated above will be provided through improvements in (i) technology,
(ii) management culture and practice, (iii) legal, policy and institutional framework; and, (iv)
linkage and interaction with other systems. The following paragraphs discusses these
improvements.
Technological benefits and impact
87.   The proposed SIAFI 21't Century project will have four major technological benefits
and impacts: (a) it will neutralize the serious risks implied in the growing obsolescense of the
current SIAFI, which may cause not only technological breakdowns and high maintenance costs,
but also related potential breakdowns of a fiscal management system largely reliant on IT



33
operational support; (b) the new technology will increase the operating efficiency of the IT and
telecommunications platforms; (c) the new technology will greatly facilitate adaptation of the
system to new legal and operating requirements, as well as the addition and integration of new
financial management functions; and, (d) linkage with other more modern government systems,
such as SIDOR and SIG will be greatly enhanced.
Impact on management culture and practice
88.   The passage from a system developed in aging programming languages and character
mode technology to a system developed for a Windows environment with strong graphic
capabilities will no doubt have a major positive impact in operations at large and in
management efficiency and effectiveness, making the retrieval and handling of information
much more friendly, faster, flexible, agile and efficient. It will permit to transform SIAFI into a
true managerial system, rather than remaining mostly an information processing and storage
system. The new environment will facilitate the integration of peripheral software to support
additional management functions at a level that not even the managerial version of SIAFI, tied to
a less flexible operating environment, can achieve today. Customization functions allowed by
the new technology will permit managers to adapt the system's outputs to their specific
information needs, which should also have an important positive impact con management culture
and practice.
Legal, policy and institutional framework
89.   As the design and development of the SIAFI 21st CENTURY application will include
functionalities directly related to PPA, LRF, asset liability/management, local government
monitoring and STN operations, it will have very positive impact on the implementation of the
relative legal and policy frameworks, as well as on institutional arrangements thereon, both as a
source of customized information and as a management tool.
Linkage with other systems
90.   A number of key government systems, including SIDOR and SIG were developed several
years after the original development of SIAFI, thus using more modem technology. This results
in limitations for the exchange of information and reduces the potential and the scope of
operating integration of the government's financial management systems.  The technological
and functional upgrading proposed under the SIAFI 21St Century project will not only facilitate
the development of more flexible, agile and efficient links, but provides the opportunity to
incorporate many such links into the new system's design, in particular in what relates to more
integration to SIDOR and SIG, which with SIAFI are the cornerstones of the Brazilian new
financial management system. It will also improve the support to SFC fiscalization and audit
activities. Navigation in the new SIAFI system for purposes such as internal and external audit as
well as to provide information to the general public as part of public sector transparency
initiatives, will also be greatly facilitated.
91.   Debt Manazement. The proposed improvements to the debt management practice in
Brazil will have an impact on the following dimensions of cost, risk, and efficiency:



34
Improved sovereign debt management will reduce the vulnerability of the debt portfolio to
financial risks, which include exchange rate and interest rate risks. The potential sources of
these risks are potentially both internal and external to the economy. Recent developments in
the monetary policy area, such as the shift from exchange rate targeting to inflation rate
targeting in the first half of 1999, and the large share of floating rate and dollar indexed
instruments have increased the vulnerability of the public debt to these financial risks. Public
debt management based on an optimal balance sheet approach which focuses on the
sovereign's risk/return tradeoff would provide the appropriate framework for risk
management under these circumstances.
* The proposed improvements to Brazil's debt management practice would also improve the
level of predictability to debt service, which is one of the major expenditure components of
the budget, thereby producing significant gains in the efficiency of budget management. The
level of predictability is improved because the desired debt portfolio structure will be defined
in a more systematic way, then a concrete action plan established in order to achieve the set
parameters. This also allows the authorities to make more precise predictions of the impact
of debt service on the budget.
The level of predictability will be further improved by the adaptation of up-to-date modeling
tools, such as stress scenario analysis and Monte-Carlo simulations, which analyzes multiple
scenarios in a very cost efficient way. These techniques allow the modeling of extreme
scenarios, through which Brazilian authorities will be able to predict and therefor prepare for
circumstances under which debt service could become unmanageable.  It is precisely
situations when the debt service becomes unmanageable that can result in a general financial
crisis, leading to sharp declines in growth and increases in poverty, and hence the ability to
predict and prepare for such situations by modeling the effect of different public debt
structures is a crucial component of the present program.
* The proposed improvements in Brazil's debt management practice can also reduce the
funding cost in the medium term by improving the credibility of the issuer. The proposed
improvements are based on the principles of clear set of objectives for managing the
government's debt, a satisfactory cost and risk framework, specific portfolio and risk
management policies, and further centralization of the government debt management
organization. These principles form the basis for sound debt management practice in
developed countries as well as for recent reforms in several countries. They improve Brazil's
credibility as an issuer by reducing uncertainty and increasing transparency of debt
management operations. They assure market participants that the government debt portfolio
is being managed prudently and efficiently, and thus allow reductions to the risk premium,
and hence funding cost, associated with poor management. Funding cost is further reduced
because the proposed upgrade of analytical tools allows the debt authorities to better manage
its funding strategy, therefore allowing them to avoid risk that a disproportionate amount of
data matures and must be refinanced at a time when market interest rates are at a cyclical
peak.
* The proposed debt management reforms improves the governance of the sovereign debt
management, making the delegation more precise and transparent and improving the
accountability of the different participants. The governance framework is grounded in a clear



35
legal framework that clarifies the authority to borrow and to issue new debt, invest, and
undertake transactions on the government's behalf.
The organizational framework for debt management needs to be well specified, and ensure
that mandates and roles are well articulated. Legal arrangements should be supported by
delegation of appropriate authority to debt managers. Experience suggests that there is a
range of institutional alternatives for allocating sovereign debt management functions across
one or more agencies, but regardless of which approach is chosen, the key requirement is to
ensure that the organizational framework surrounding debt management is clearly specified,
there is coordination and sharing of information, and that the mandates of the respective
players are clear.
* The government can reduce the cost and risk of the public debt portfolio over the medium
run by further developing the domestic bond market. This is particularly important for Brazil
given the importance of the domestic debt stock. The proposed development of the domestic
bond market and the consolidation of Brazil's international yield curve results in important
externalities to the whole economy, private as well as other public issuers.  At the
macroeconomic policy level, it reduces the potentially dangerous reliance on foreign
currency denominated debt. Debt service costs over the medium to long term will decrease
through the development of a deep and liquid market. At the more microeconomic level,
development of a domestic debt market increases overall financial stability and improves
fimancial intermediation through greater competition and development of related financial
infrastructure, products, and services. The development of domestic markets and a related
benchmark yield curve facilitates the introduction of new products, including repurchase
agreements (repos), money market instruments, structured finance, and derivatives, that can
improve risk management and financial stability.
SUSTAINABILITY OF THE REFORMS
92.   Fiscal Responsibility Law. The odds that the reforms will remain are good, but better in
some areas than in others. While it is difficult to put a number to the probability that the reforms
not be reversed, it is possible to point to the factors that make this probability higher than 50%.
With regard to the Fiscal Responsibility Law, these factors are quite clear: they include the
country's commitment to low inflation, which requires fiscal sustainability, and the contribution
of the Law to a stronger budget constraint and to an orderly budget process (though the ex-ante
constraint and the ex-post conciliation mechanisms described before), both of which aid fiscal
sustainability. The country's commitment to low inflation is reflected in the political success of
price stabilization through the Plano Real and the sophisticated and widely publicized inflation
targeting framework put in place last year. This framework can be successful only if monetary
financing of the public sector is contained enough to accommodate targeting of stable prices.
This requires a framework that keeps primary balances high enough to avoid unsustainable debt
levels (levels of debt with debt service obligations that will not reasonably be met). The LFR
provides the budget constraint elements and the budget processes to achieve this end.
Sustainability could falter, however, under an scenario of excessive demands for expenditures
(say, exploding retirement benefit obligations) that breaks down the commitment to price
stability and fiscal orderliness. This risk is, as discussed in the next chapter, moderate.



36
93.   Multiannual Planning Framework With regard to the PPA process, the factors that
support sustainability are not as sharp as in the case of the Fiscal Responsibility Law. Fiscal
reform is never as simple as issuing a decree or passing a law. A PPA type of reform is specially
challenging. Organizational and institutional change face enormous hurdles, including
overcoming tradition and habit, and breaking established alliances between Ministry activities,
interest groups, and political parties.
94.   Nevertheless, the probability of the new PPA process going forward are enhanced beyond
chance by (i) the potential benefits of the reforms, (ii) the Government's awareness of the
difficulty of the PPA challenge, (iii) the government's commitment to the reform, and (iv) the
emerging institutionalization of the PPA. The first of these factors was discussed above (paras.
78-80). Those benefits (essentially better allocation and management use of public resources)
are an important incentive to go forward with the PPA approach. On the government's awareness
of the difficulties of implementing a PPA approach, the Ministry of Planning staff leading the
PPA reforms recognize the difficulty of their task, and have tried to take into account some of the
hurdles they face in implementing the reforms. They are under no illusion of the difficulty of
their task. This pragmatism and realism among staff at the Planning Ministry is a positive factor
in assessing the sustainability of reforms.
95.   The Government's commitment to the PPA approach is reflected in the significant
efforts it has invested in advancing the approach and its associated public sector reforms. These
efforts include:
- developing an inventory of all activities in the Government
*  arranging activities by common objectives into programs
*  developing physical performance indicators and targets for each program and activity
3  assigning program managers to each program
*  establishing a training institute for prograrn managers and providing intensive training
*  preparing and submitting the 2001 Budget by program
*  undertaking high-level policy process to identify strategic programs within the budget
3  developing a computerized system for monitoring program execution
*  developing a PPA evaluation survey to feedback from program managers on PPA
This level of effort is indicative of the importance the Government has placed in PPA, and the
likelihood that the Government will press ahead with implementing PPA reforms.
96.   Institutionalization is strengthened by the PPA's constitutional mandate. While the
various reforms, such as performance management, are not Constitutional requirements, the PPA
planning document must be produced. Certainly PPA will remain in place for the foreseeable
future. Given that many of the PPA reforms, such as performance monitoring and program
structure support setting priorities through the PPA process, it is likely that future Governments
would find the reforms useful to keep, as the process it makes it easier for them to set their own
priorities.
97.   Regarding institutionalization of the reforms, given that this is the first year of the PPA's
operation, not too much can be expected. PPA has not become routine practice or accepted
practice among the bureaucracy. Nonetheless, there are some aspects of PPA that indicate a
degree of permanence to the reform. For example:



37
*  The existence of several hundred trained program managers across the Government.
*  The PPA reforms are being lead by a permanent Ministry (Planning) rather than a temporary
task force.
*  The inclusion of the PPA evaluation reform in law (The LFR includes a requirement for
programs to be evaluated).
*  The requirement that an annual report on PPA progress be submitted to Congress, which
supports evaluation and performance management reforms.
98.   On the other hand, having indicated the factors that raise the likelihood of the PPA being
sustained, the potential risks to the sustainability and to the achievement of its full potential
benefits must be acknowledged. These risks are important and are discussed in the risk section
further below (para. 132).
99.   Financial Management System. Once in place, the new SIAFI system can be expected
to have stronger long-term sustainability, as its technological environment will be state-of-the-art
from its very beginning compared to the current SIAFI system which was developed with
technology that at the time was about to complete its expected life cycle. There are three main
types of arguments for this appraisal referring to: (i) technology; (ii) management culture; and
(iii) the legal, policy and institutional framework.
Technological considerations
100.  The proposed distinct three-layer architecture (data base, application and user interface)
of the new system will largely facilitate the introduction of new technologies at each level as
they become available, without a general disruption of the system as a whole, which is a major
problem that the current SIAFI is presently confronting. The proposed use of CASE-based
design and development and a graphic Windows environment will result in an application that
can be easily adapted to future changes in financial management legislation, processes and other
institutional arrangements, in particular if advanced application customization capabilities and
tools are incorporated in its design.
Management culture
101.  From a functional perspective, Brazil has accumulated 13 years of integrated financial
management experience supported in the widespread use of SIAFI by all federal government
spending units. An administrative culture for IT-based financial management culture has been
developed and is firmly established, supporting the expectation that the new SIAFI will be
sustainable as a system.
Legal, policy and institutional framework
102.  The updating of SIAFI is not being proposed solely in response to the growing
technological obsolescence of the current systemr, but principally in order to adapt the system to
major changes that are taking place under Brazil's far reaching fiscal reform program. SIAFI
21st century will specifically address the monitoring and evaluation requirements of the PPA and
the LRF, as well as the introduction of new permanent institutional arrangements for enhanced
public debt management and federal government asset/liability management. The new system
will be one of the major sources of information for program managers under the PPA, and will



38
specifically monitor the federal and local governments' compliance with the fiscal transparency
requirements under the LRF. New institutional arrangements for public debt management and
asset/liability management will be specifically addressed in the new system both from an
information standpoint as well as for operations. Therefore, not being an stand-alone
technological project but being intimately immersed in the framework of major fiscal reform
developments, it is reasonable to expect that SIAFI 21st century will be a sustainable long-range
proposition.
103.  Debt Institutions. A major factor that determines the sustainability of a debt policy is
the structure of the debt portfolio. Traditional debt sustainability frameworks which exclusively
focuses on the relative size of the public debt are inadequate when the composition of the debt
portfolio significantly alters its cost/risk tradeoff. Poorly structured debt has been a factor in
inducing or propagating recent economic crises. Although perhaps they are not the sole cause of
these crises, the maturity structure, interest rate and currency composition, and obligations due to
contingent liabilities of the government's debt portfolio have often contributed to the severity of
the crisis. Excessive focus on possible savings associated with large volumes of short-term debt
seriously exposes the government budgets to changing financial market conditions, including
changes in the country creditworthiness when this debt has to be rolled over.
104.  Greater reliance on the private mnarket for government financing combined with financial
liberalization and globalization have increased the financial vulnerability of public debt
portfolios. Before these sweeping changes in the late 1980s and early 1990s changed the nature
of public debt management, government financing relied largely on the central bank financing
the debt, with the domestic banking industry often acting as a captive market for government
bonds. This relatively static environment changed dramatically as open economies became
increasingly sensitive to changes in international capital flows.
105.  With those previous government financing arrangements no longer sustainable,
governments were forced to increase its reliance on the market for its financing needs, resulting
in complex debt portfolio structures and instruments, and the potential for increased volatility.
As a result, effective government debt management now requires a more comprehensive
framework and new market-oriented skills which allow the public debt managers to adequately
deal with tasks such as risk management and hedging operations which are needed to manage the
increased financial vulnerability.
106.  The traditional institutional settings are inadequate to deal with the new challenges of
debt management, in particular for countries like Brazil with a large stock of debt and complex
portfolio structure. In the past, the institutional setting of most emerging countries were highly
bureaucratic and characterized by a slow decision-making process and often dispersed
organizational arrangements. This was adequate for a relatively static debt funding environment
which relied heavily on the Central Bank and multilateral agencies.
107.  Such institutional arrangements have proven to be inadequate for managing the
government debt portfolio under a market oriented government financing environment where
more complex decisions have to be rapidly made regarding a larger set of interrelated issues. As
a result, the trend has been to centralize and consolidate debt management in order to facilitate
the management of the public debt portfolio from a whole balance sheet perspective, and to
create an independent debt office which expedites its decision-making process by reducing



39
bureaucratic overhead. The capacity of the debt office has been increased as governments have
realized that they need to attract more highly skilled personnel who can execute market analysis
and operations. At the same time, typically the debt management responsibilities of the Central
Banks have been reduced or eliminated so that they can independently pursue monetary policies
without being undermined by fiscal policy. Changes in Brazil's debt management institutions are
consistent with these trends. Centralization of debt management at the Trasury, improved
institutions for accountability, greater capacity for policy formulation and implementation will
all help improve policy outcomes and thereby make the institutional changes sustainable
THE PROPOSED LOAN
LINK TO CAS
108.  The proposed PFRSAL (the first adjustment loan in support of the first phase of the
Program) is within the assistance program covered by the CAS discussed on March 30, 2000
(R2000-25;IFC/R2000-34). The CAS included poverty reduction and fiscal adjustment as
principal objectives, complemented by assistance programs for growth, development
effectiveness and environmental management. Within the context of these objectives, the CAS
anticipated support through programmatic lending in the area of " improvements in fiscal
institutions and public sector management for better fiscal performance and incentives for fiscal
discipline ,28. Furthermore, the proposed PFRSAL, as indicated before, is a follow-up operation
to the Fiscal and Administrative Reform Loan approved last March in that it supports further
fiscal reforms aimed at fiscal sustainability and improved public sector performance. The
proposed PRFSAL would be in the amount of US$757.58 million. The remaining assistance
under the proposed program would be $450-$750 million.
109. The CAS anticipates $1.5 billion in special adjustment lending for 2000-02, of which
$1.0 billion have already been approved and disbursed. The remaining amount of $0.5 billion of
possible special lending was dropped at the Government's request, as the emergency conditions
that justified such assistance have now subsided. The CAS also anticipates $2.0 of adjustment
lending at standard terms. The Government has requested the Bank for an additional $0.5 billion
of adjustment lending at the Bank's standard terms. The next CAS update, to be submitted in
April, 2001, will include this proposal.
PROGRAM OBJECTIVE
110.  Given the background discussed in the previous chapter, the operation described below
would support a program with the following objective: to improve Brazil's aggregate fiscal
performance, and the quality of public expenditures (better allocational and technical efficiency
and better delivery of public services), including the quality of debt service expenditures. This
2S See CAS, March 6, 2000; Report Number 20160-BR, p. 31.



40
objective falls within the CAS objectives of development and implementation of structural
reforms and fiscal improvement. Pursuit of these objectives continues to be urgent to sustain the
gains that Brazil has achieved by reducing inflation and implementing the Government's rigorous
fiscal adjustment programn during 1999-2000.
PROGRAM DESCRIPTION
111.  The Program consists of policy actions to improve fiscal performance, public sector
expenditure management, and debt management chiefly through continued implementation of
the Government's primary surplus and debt targets (the Fiscal Stability Program), deepening of
Fiscal Discipline (through the new Fiscal Responsibility Law), a better design of the public
expenditure planning and implementation process (through the new PPA and improved
monitoring of expenditures); and more professional debt management (through better debt
management institutions). The actions are summarized in Annex 2. They are classified into three
of the four areas discussed in the previous chapter (fiscal, expenditure, and debt management.
Tax reform is excluded because it is not expected to materialize within the PFRSAL horizon
(2000-02). If passed, it could  be              Box 8: Possible Technical Assistance

114. The Bank's financial assistance will help Brazil meet its external financing requirements,
while the proposed reform program supports medium-term fiscal adjustment. During the year
2001, Brazil's current account deficit balance is expected to amount to about $24.8 billion, of
which $5.0 billion are expected to come from multilateral institutions. Moreover, the loan will
also help meet the federal government's financing requirement of about R$15 billion in 2001,
and will also help lengthen the term structure of the public sector's debt, which would reduce
vulnerability to financial shocks.
115. The financial assistance provided by the proposed PFRSAL will be complemented by a
TA project that would include four components: (i) assistance in the implementation of the LFR
(e.g., in governments' various obligations under the LFR); (ii) assistance in the implementation
of the PPA (e.g., in methods of program evaluation) (iii) assistance in the proposed development
of the SIAFI 21st century program; and (iv) assistance in the improvement of debt management
institutions. Box 8 provides some details. This project is presently at the preparation stage.
PROGRAM IMPLEMENTATION AND SUPERVISION
116.  Supervision will review progress of the implementation of proposed program in each of
its four areas. It will focus on (a) the implementation of the reforms; and (b) the outcomes of the
reforms. The review will largely be based on the monitoring indicators and the goals of the
reforms. The reforms and their implementation dimensions have been discussed throughout this
report. The reforms supported by the proposed operation are summarized in Annex 2, together
with the key actions that are expected for the second loan, their intermediate monitoring
indicators (which serve as benchmarks to assess whether to proceed with a 2ndor 3rd loan), and
the goals that the reforms pursue. Table 4 summarizes the indicators and their expected values,
which serve as benchmarks. These indicators and the possible benchmarks have been indicated
by the Government in its Letter of Development Policy (Annex 1), and are explained as
follows:
*  MIacroeconomic Performance: Key indicators are growth, inflation, and the PSBR. The
numbers in Table 4 reflect the Government's expectations of those indicators. One of these -
inflation -has been formalized through the government's inflation targeting framework
introduced last year.
*  Fiscal Stability: The indicators - the public sector's primary balance and net debt - have
been targeted by the government as part of its 1998 Fiscal Stability Program, which runs
through 2001. The Federal Government's primary balance and debt beyond 2001 have been
indicated in the Government's Budget Guidelines Law.
*  Fiscal Planning: (i) Under the LFR, governments (all states and municipalities with
population exceeding 50.000) must include Fiscal Target and Risk annexes in their LDOs for



42
2002 budgets. This is at the heart of the fiscal planning process that provides an ex-ante
constraint on budget programming. The information on LDOs will be useful to assess the
fiscal stance of the public sector. The Government is planning on developing a report
(perhaps with IPEA) with consolidation and analysis of public sector fiscal prospects in
LDOs. Production of this report provides an indicator of progress in fiscal planning of the
overall public sector.
*  Transparency: Under the LFR, (i) governments (from all states and municipalities with
population exceeding 50,000) must disseminate PPAs, LDOs, Budgets, Public Accounts,
budget execution summary reports, and Fiscal Management Reports; (ii) the Federal
Government must create a Registry of Public Sector Debt at STN. This is a challenge, given
existing informational gaps on sub-national debts. Hence creation of the registry is an
important benchmark of progress; (iii) the Federal Government must consolidate public
accounts from all governments. This consolidation will be a major step forward in public
information of public budgets and an indicator of progress in the implementation of the Law.
*  PPA Evaluation System: The Government intends to undertake a review of its program
evaluation procedures (this would be part of the companion TA operation). Indicators of
progress in this undertaking include consultant reports and the revised operational procedures
that result from such a review.
*  Integration of planning/evaluation to budgeting: (i) The Government is undertaking a review
of programs and management, based on reporting by PPA program managers. Production of
this report provides an indicator of PPA program and management assessment, including
PPA management assessment methods. The LDO (2002 budget) adjustment of budget
priorities provides an indication of the bearing of program evaluation on priorities;
* SIAFI 21't Century: The SIAFI upgrading is organized around four modules (budget,
financial, patrimonial, STN), during a 2001-02 horizon. An indicator of progress is given by
effective use of the completed modules by government spending units.
*  Administrative Reform: The indicator is approval of the Inadequate Performance Dismissal
Bill, the last pending piece of legislation required to make administrative reform operational.
*  Debt Management : Indicators of debt policy performance will also be monitored. They
cover progress in governance (i) transfer of external debt operations to the Treasury has been
concluded; (ii) issued code of conduct for treasury officials; (iii) issued debt management
guidelines and procedures)]; capacity building: [(i) regular middle office reports on the debt
portfolio and STN operations; (ii) annual borrowing plan disclosed; benchmark
development: (annual borrowing plan, initial benchmark and medium term debt strategy
established).
117.  Supervision will also cover reform actions other than the key actions indicated in Table 4.
Other actions are summarized in the Annex 3, which provides a broader account of the
Government's overall fiscal reform program. In addition to actions, supervision will also focus
on an assessment of outcomes. This covers, inter alia macroeconomic variables (e.g., growth,
inflation, the current account); other fiscal variables (the PSBR, federal and sub-national fiscal
performance; meeting of targets under the PPA; evolution of personnel expenditures, and



43
indicators of debt management performance, (e.g. average maturity, duration, foreign exchange
debt, secondary market liquidity, and average cost of debt).

DISBURSEMENT AND AUDITING
118.  The loan would disburse under the Bank's provisions for fast disbursing operations,
which require no procurement of goods or services. The flow of the proceeds form the loan
would be as follows:
* The Bank deposits the proceeds in a Govermment's account at Banco do Brasil, New York;



44
* The proceeds enter into the Central Bank of Brazil's International Reserves Account,
denominated in dollars;
*  The Central Bank enters the counterpart proceeds, denominated in domestic currency, into
the Government's account at the Central Bank (the Conta Unica do Tesouro). This account
earns the average yield of Treasury bills at the Central Bank;
*  The Conta Unica do Tesouro has a classification of funds by revenue source. The domestic
currency proceeds at the Government's account at the Central Bank (Conta Unica do
Tesouro) are classified as Resources from Foreign Credit (account item No. 148);
*  Expenditure of the proceeds from the loan at the Conta Unica do Tesouro is earmarked, in
the Federal Budget, for repayment of federal external debt;
*  To repay debt, the Government uses the earmarked domestic currency proceeds in its Central
Bank account to purchase the required dollars from a bank;
*  The procedures described above are subject to Ministry of Finance internal controls
(Secretaria Federal de Control or SFC) and to external controls by the Federal Accounts
Tribunal.
The Bank may require audits of the account into which the proceeds of the loan will be
disbursed.
PROGRAM BENEFITS
119.  The overall benefits of the program would be to improve Brazil's performance on
growth, poverty reduction and public service delivery to its citizens. Direct benefits of the
program components include: (i) continued aggregate fiscal balance; (ii) improved fiscal
planning, transparency, and performance incentives, including stronger incentives for sub-
national creditworthiness; (iii) an improved federal financial management system, (iv) improved
incentives for civil service performance; (v) a long term expenditure framework (the PPA),
closely linked to the budget, and (vi) quality of debt management raised to international best
practice for large open economies. These direct benefits translate into the more fundamental aims
of fiscal sustainability, allocative and technical efficiency of public expenditures, and better
public services to Brazil's citizens. Para. 74 above indicated how the different parts of the
program may contribute to these aims. These are aims that ultimately have a bearing on
economic welfare. Fiscal sustainability implies an eventual reduction in debt levels that will help
reduce real interest rates and increase economic growth. Increased allocative and technical
efficiency of public services has a direct bearing on economic welfare of the population. Also,
given the importance of education and health in public expenditures, these services play a direct
role in poverty reduction.
LESSONS LEARNED
120.  There has been much international experience in the areas covered by the Government's
fiscal reform program. In the area relating to the fiscal codes like the LFR, existing academic
research has focused on the role of both political institutions and budget institutions. As
discussed in Annex 4, research in political institutions suggests fiscal stability is more
challenging in political systems that, like Brazil's, have a high degree of proportionality in
representation. Although Brazil's well-established democracy, with a standard length of a
government duration, favors fiscal discipline, its system of proportional representation (with



45
three senators per state, at least eight representatives per municipality, and five major parties)
makes consensus building more challenging, particularly under conditions of weak party
29
discipline.9
