51.   By any standard, the scope and the cost of the SIAFI 21st Century are no doubt too
critical and large for the Brazilian government to undertake the risk of initiating the project
without firmly securing its full financing. Otherwise, completion risks for the project
would increase significantly. Considering the tight implementation schedule of the project,
which requires implementation to begin January 2001, it would seem necessary that
immediate actions be taken by the SNT and SERPRO to secure full project financing, in
addition to reviewing the project's budget. Another urgent requirement would be to put in
place no later than the beginning of 2001 an accountable and strong project management
team. Support on project management is one of the areas that would be addresses in the TA
loan currently under preparation.
IMPLEMENTATIONAND COMPLETIONRISKS
52.   Like any large systems development project, SIAFI 21't Century faces important
implementation and completion risks, for which there are a number of equally important
mitigating factors.
53.   Technolo2ical risks. From the technological standpoint, consideration is being
given to models, platforms, environments and development techniques and tools which
represent a dramatic departure from those prevailing since the current SIAFI system was
developed and implemented some 13 years ago. This will have major effects on
information resources both, during project execution and later for post-implementation
maintenance. System users will be migrating from a rigid character mode interface
environment to a graphic mode Windows and internet environments. System availability
for 24 hours per day, 7 days per week, while being a benefit, will also represent work
scheduling challenges both for information resources and users.
54.   However, technological risks are mitigated by the careful approach being taken by
both, the STN and SERPRO in terms of validating technological alternatives and choices
through extensive pilot testing at each of the three proposed system layers (data, application
and user interface) with the support of external consultants and the participation of potential



105
suppliers of goods and services. Other mitigating factors include current training of
information resources in the use of the design and development tools that will be used in
the construction of applications, as well as plans to provide extensive training to users of
the systems before the various subsystems become operational. Another consideration is
that while the technology being considered is new in a SIAFI environment, there is
substantial successful experience with it, both internationally and in Brazil. Finally,
SERPRO has a very solid and long standing experience as a developer and operator of
government systems in Brazil, including the development of the current SIAFI system and
its operation during the past 13 years, thus it is familiar with the Brazilian legal framework
and rules of the business for public sector financial management at all federal government
levels.
55.   Political and financing risks. The risk of changes in political priorities are always
higher in the case of multi-annual projects requiring high levels of investment.
Technological and/or financial risks may materialize producing delays and cost overruns in
project implementation which could result in a loss of political favor and completion
funding. Coordination between project completion and the budgeting and financial
schedules may be lost because of technological or funding delays, resulting in project
implementation breakdowns. General economic and financial conditions may force a
government to cut down on new and ongoing projects, specially when they are as large as
the new SIAFI project. A new administration may decide to pursue a different approach for
a project that has a high-profile incidence over government management systems,
transparency and accountability.
56.   Notwithstanding these and other completion risks, the SIAFI 21st Century project is
being proposed not only as a much needed technological development in view of the
increasing obsolescence of the current SIAFI, but it is also supported on major policy
actions, such as the PPA and the Fiscal Responsibility Law (LRF), which are key
components of the current administration's program. The project is scheduled to be
completed during the current administration's term in office. Being instrumental to
facilitate monitoring and evaluation of performance under the PPA and the LRF, it is likely
to remain funded until completion, though implementation delays taking completion
beyond the current administration's term could have less predictable effects. The support
being provided by the Bank to PPA and LRF implementation can also be considered as a
completion risk mitigating factor. The project's implementation schedule is based on the
gradual completion and implementation of its various subsystems and modules, thus there
is built-in flexibility to establish development priorities in coordination with budgeting and
financial fiscal year schedules. The fact that subsystems or modules of the current SIAFI
will continue operating as needed during the implementation of the project substantially
mitigates the risk of breakdowns in financial management if the project faces delays or
completion problems.
57.   In summnary, while SIAFI 21st Century faces the various types of implementation
and completion risks inherent to a project of its type and scope, there are also important
mitigating factors that support its viability, among which stand out the sound approach
reportedly being taken by STN and SERPRO for the validation of technological alternatives
and the fact that the project falls within the framework of major international agency



106
supported policy initiatives such as the PPA and the LRF. In order to further mitigate
project risks, a similar approach should be evidenced in relation of the financial validation
of the project.
SUSTAINABILITYOFSIAFI21sf CENTURY
58.   Once in place, the new SIAFI system can be expected to have stronger long-term
sustainability, as it its technological environment will be state-of-the-art from its very
beginning compared to the current SIAFI system which was developed with technology
that at the time was about to complete its expected life cycle. There are three main types of
arguments for this appraisal:
(a) Technological;
(b) Management culture; and,
(c) Legal, policy and institutional arrangements framework
59.   Technological considerations. The proposed distinct three-layer architecture (data
base, application and user interface) of the new system will largely facilitate the
introduction of new technologies at each level as they become available, without a general
disruption of the system as a whole, which is a major problem that the current SIAFI is
presently confronting. The proposed use of CASE-based design and development and a
graphic Windows environment will result in an application that can be easily adapted to
future changes in financial management legislation, processes and other institutional
arrangements, in particular if advanced application customization capabilities and tools are
incorporated in its design..
60.   Manag-ement culture. From a functional perspective, Brazil has accumulated 13
years of integrated financial management experience supported in the widespread use of
SIAFI by all federal government spending units. An administrative culture for IT-based
fmancial management has been developed and is firmly established, supporting the
expectation that the new SIAFI will be sustainable as a system.
61.   Legal, policy and institutional arrangements framework   As previously
mentioned the updating of SIAFI is not being proposed solely in response to the growing
technological obsolescence of the current system, but principally in order to adapt the
system to major changes that are taking place under Brazil's far reaching fiscal reform
program. SIAFI 21st Century will specifically address the monitoring and evaluation
requirements of the PPA and the LRF, as well as the introduction of new permanent
institutional arrangements for enhanced public debt management and federal government
asset/liability management.  The new system will be one of the major sources of
information for program managers under the PPA, and will specifically monitor federal and
local governments compliance with the fiscal transparency requirements of under the LRF.
New institutional arrangements for public debt management and asset/liability management
will be specifically addressed in the new system both, from an information standpoint as
well as for operations. Therefore, not being an stand-alone technological project but being



107
intimately immersed in the framework of major fiscal reform developments, it is
reasonable to expect that SIAFI 215t Century will be a sustainable long-range proposition.
EXPECTED BENEFITS AND IMPACT OF THE SIAFI 21s' CENTURY PROPOSAL
62.   As for the assessment of its sustainability, the benefits and the impact of the SIAFI
21"t Century project can also be established in terms of:
(a) Technology;
(b) Management culture and practice; and,
(c) Legal, policy and institutional arrangements framework; and,
(d) Linkage and interaction with other systems.
63.   Technoloieical benefits and impact. In addition to specific benefits discussed in
section 4.2 of this Annex, the proposed SIAFI 21st Century project will have four major
technological benefits and impacts: (a) it will neutralize the serious risks implied in the
growing obsolescence of the current SIAFI, which may cause not only technological
breakdowns and high maintenance costs, but also related potential breakdowns of a fiscal
management system largely reliant on IT operational support; (b) the new technology will
increase the operating efficiency of the IT and telecommunications platforms; (c) the new
technology will greatly facilitate adaptation of the system to new legal and operating
requirements, as well as the addition and integration of new financial management
functions; and, (d) linkage with other more modern government systems, such as SIDOR
and SIG will be greatly enhanced.
64.   Manai-ement culture and practice benefits and impact. The passage from a
system developed in aging programming languages and character mode technology to a
system developed for a Windows environment with strong graphic capabilities will no
doubt have a major positive impact in operations at large and in management efficiency
and effectiveness, making the retrieval and handling of information much more friendly,
faster, flexible, agile and efficient. It will permit to transform SIAFI into a true managerial
system, rather than remaining mostly an information processing and storage system. The
new environment will facilitate the integration of peripheral software to support additional
management functions at a level that not even the managerial version of SIAFI, tied to a
less flexible operating environment, can achieve today. Customization functions allowed
by the new technology will permit managers to adapt the system's outputs to their specific
information needs, which should also have an important positive impact con management
culture and practice.
65.   Legal, policy and institutional arrangements framework and impact. As the
design and development of the SIAFI 21St Century application will include functionalities
directly related to PPA, LRF, asset liability/management, local government monitoring and
STN operations, it will have very positive impact on the implementation of the relative
legal and policy frameworks, as well as on institutional arrangements thereon, both as a
source of customized information and as a management tool.



108
66.   Linkage with other systems. A number of key government systemns, including
SIDOR and SIG were developed several years after the original development of SIAFI,
thus using more modern technology. This results in limitations for the exchange of
information and reduces the potential and the scope of operating integration of the
government's financial management systems. The technological and functional upgrading
proposed under the SIAFI 21t Century project will not only facilitate the development of
more flexible, agile and efficient links, but provides the opportunity to incorporate many
such links into the new system's design, in particular in what relates to more integration to
SIDOR and SIG, which with SIAFI are the cornerstones of the Brazilian new financial
management system. It will also improve the support to SFC fiscalization and audit
activities. Navigation in the new SIAFI system for purposes such as internal and external
audit as well as to provide information to the general public as part of public sector
transparency initiatives, will also be greatly facilitated.
CONSISTENCY WITH LESSONS LEARNED AND INTERNATIONAL
EXPERIENCES
67.   The SIAFI 21st CENTURY proposal is largely consistent with lessons learned in
Brazil in relation to the management of large governmental information systems, as well as
with international experiences in relation to the normal life cycle of integrated financial
management applications in the public sector.
68.   An important lesson learned is that systems have to be flexible to accommodate
various types of users, and that their technology must permit adaptation without major
structural changes or programming to new legal and policy environments and to ever
changing institutional arrangements. A large centralized mainframe system like the current
SIAFI, accessible mainly by terminals does not provide these facilities. Because of this, the
"management version" of SIAFI had to be eventually implemented and there is not
adequate and efficient integration between its original and more recent subsystems. These
and other lessons are being taken into account for the design and development of the new
SIAFI 21st Century.
69.   The normal life span of IFMS systems in Latin-American countries comparable to
Brazil y size and complexity of their federal systems has been much less than SIAFI's 13
years in operation. The Argentine SIDIF system was developed around 1992 also using
computing languages and environments that even then were close to obsolescence. SIDIF
was subject to comprehensive technological and functional modernization in the late
nineties, including the development of institutional or "local" SIDIF version to be
implemented in ministries and other entities finances with federal budget resources. In the
case of Mexico, the SIAF application developed and implemented in the federal treasury
department in the early nineties has been recently replaced by a new, more modern version.
Therefore, the modernization of SIAFI is in line with processes observed in other
comparable countries in the region, and is even past due by some 4 to 5 years.
70.   From a functional standpoint, new IFMS systems are normally developed, as will be
the case for SIAFI 215t Century, with stronger debt management facilities, improvements in
telecommunications support, including the use of internet by financial management units



109
and providing more access to civil society organizations and the general public, reflecting
increase demands for government transparency.
71.   However, from a financial standpoint the project's budget does not seem to be in
line with international experiences observed in countries such as Argentina and Mexico,
though in order to confirm this assessment, more pointed comparative analysis would be
required in areas such as the comparative  deployment of the systems and their
technological and functional scopes. Also, the project's proposed budget should be further
validated as discussed above.
THE BRAZILL4 NAUDIT PROCESS
72.   External and internal control and audit are necessary complements of sound
financial management, program management and accountability, thus it is important to
review the relevant control and audit processes when discussing and evaluating SIAFI, the
PPA and the LRF.
73.   At the federal level, external control and audit in Brazil is the responsibility of the
Tribunal de Contas da Uniao (TCU), which follows a tribunal organizational and
functional structure rather than that of an office of the comptroller general (OCG) as in
several Latin American countries. Internal control and audit within the federal executive
falls under the Federal Internal Control System (SCI) and its governing body the Secretaria
Federal de Control (SFC) of the MOF.
External control and audit
74.   Governing body. The TCU seats in Brasilia, and is integrated by 9 magistrates or
ministers, six being appointed by Congress and three by the Executive Branch. Two of the
magistrates selected by the Executive Branch must be either auditors or public prosecutors
of the TCU. The TCU is organized like a court of law in a plenary and two chambers, each
of these being integrated by four ministers and one or two auditors, depending on the
characteristics of each case. The TCU is supported by five technical units or secretariats,
including one for external control and another for internal control.  The other three
technical units handle administration, the TCU's session schedule, and auditing training.
Under the co-ordination of the secretary for external control, the five technical secretaries
of the TUC integrate a permanent consultative commission which supports the TCU's
magistrates in matters related to the planning, organization and methods, human resources
administration, budget, patrimony, and technology pertaining the TCU. The federal
Constitution provides for an specialized and autonomous public prosecution office
(Ministerio Publico) within the TCU's organic structure, integrated by an attorney general,
three deputy attorney generals and four attorneys appointed by the Executive Branch, with
responsibility for the defense of public order, the economic interests of the state, and to
procure justice. The Ministerio Publico can also express opinion on all matters brought
before the TCU.



110
75.   Legal framework and functional responsibilities of the TCU.  The federal
Constitution establishes that the TCU has functional responsibilities for the evaluation
fiscalization and audit of:
* The annual accounts of the executive;
* The accounts of administrators and other parties responsible for public monies, goods
and assets;
* The legality of acts whereby public sector staff is hired and retirement conditions are
granted, as well as reforms to civil and military pensions;
* The accounts of state companies;
* The application of federal revenues transferred to states and municipalities, including
the federal district;
* The application of subsidies and the waiving of revenues in the public sector;
76.   The Constitution also provides for the following actions to be performed by the
TCU:
* The application of sanctions and the correction of legal violations and irregularities in
public acts and contracts;
- Provide information to Congress on its fiscalization actions;
- At the request of Congress, issue a conclusive pronouncement on non-authorized public
sector expenditures;
- Process accusations made by citizens, political parties, associations or unions on legal
violations and irregularities in the public sector;
-  Fix the coefficients for the distribution or participations of federal revenues to states,
the federal district and municipalities, and fiscalize their transfer to the local
governments.
77.   Additionally, the organic law of the TCU (Law nbr. 8443, July 16, 1992 and its
subsequent reforms) establishes the following main responsibilities:
* Resolve consultations made by government authorities about the application of legal or
statutory norms in any area under the TCU's purview;
* Control the legality of the assets and revenues of government authorities and public
servants;
* Evaluate the privatization of public enterprises included in the national decentralization
program;
* Evaluate representations made by participants in government bidding processes about
irregularities in the application of the federal procurement law (Lei de Licitacoes e
Contratos);
* Evaluate representations made by municipal authorities about irregularities in the
transfer of federal revenues earmarked to local governments.



II1
The organic law of the TCU and its internal estatutes (Regimento Interno do TCU, June
15, 1993) and its many subsequent reforms, establish in detail the scope of each
fiscalization action, including basic norms for external control, including the
performance at the TCU's own initiative, the performance of formal inspections and
external audits.   The TCU's organic law also defines the framework for internal
control and audits, pursuant to constitutional provisions.
78.   Institutional arrangements for external control and audit. Within the TCU, its
technical secretariat for external control (SECEX) is in charge of fiscalization and audit. In
turn, SECEX is internally supported by technical units specializing in the planning and
management of the fiscalization and audit processes, in the execution of control actions and
audits, and in information technology. The TCU has 37 external controls and audit
administration units throughout the country, operating under the purview of its technical
secretariat for external control (SECEX). 26 of these units are located in state capitals,
while 11 operate in Brasilia. They are responsible for all inspection work inherent to
external control and for the performance of formal audits. More specifically, of the units
located in Brasilia, 7 monitor accounts and management actions and perform formal
external audits in assigned groups of federal entities, three are in charge of specialized
control areas, such as civil service, pensions and government decentralization including
privatizations, government accounts, transfers of revenues to local governments and
government revenues, and the remaining unit supports the administration of processes
brought to the attention of the TCU. Units in state capitals perform external control and
audit functions that are relevant at the state level, and are also responsible for the
fiscalization of the application of public resources by municipal governments and other
state and municipal entities. The TCU also has a training branch (Instituto Serzedello
Correa), who supports the human resource recruitment and the technical development of
auditors and develops research activities in the control and audit area.
Internal control and audit
79.   Governing body. The Secretaria Federal de Controle (SFC) is responsible for the
federal government's Internal Control System (SCI). SFC was created in April 1994, under
the MOF. Article 74 of the federal constitution provides for the establishment of the SCI
and assigns it the following functions:
*  Evaluating compliance with the targets of the multi-annual plan (PPA) in relation to the
execution of the government's prograrns and the federal budget;
*  Establishing the legality and validate results in relation to efficiency and effectiveness
of budgetary, financial and patrimonial management in all federal administration
entities, as well as in relation to the application of public resources by civil entities;
*  Controlling credit operations, including guarantees, as well as assets and liabilities of
the union; and,
* Providing support to external control activities both, through the performance of audits,
issuing audit reports and certifications, and through formal requests to administrative
authorities to undertake special audits whenever  illegalities or irregularities are
observed.



112
80.   More recently, the federal government has updated the framework of the SCI by
way of Decree Nbr. 3.591, of September 6, 2000, which   redefines its  functional,
organizational and jurisdictional structures, and also establishes rules and standards on a
number of specific, general and transitory internal control issues. While the Constitutional
purposes of the SCI remain unchanged, the following internal control activities are
specified in the new Decree:
* Evaluation of compliance with the program  targets established in the PPA and of its
execution;
* Evaluation of the execution of the government's programs, to determine the level of
execution of their respective targets, the achievement of outputs and outcomes, and
management performance;
* Evaluation of the execution of the federal budgets, in order to determine compliance
with the limits and appropriations established by applicable legislation;
* Evaluation of the performance of federal public administrators, in order to determine
the legality of their actions and to evaluate results in terms of economy, efficiency and
effectiveness of budgetary, financial, patrimonial and human resources mnanagement,
and other administrative and operating systems;
* Control of credit and guarantee operations, as well as operations related to the Union's
rights and assets, in order to establish their consistency and their compliance with
internal controls;
81.   Internal control activities have also been redefined as being supportive of public
sector management at all levels, as well as of enhancements in the formulation, planning,
coordination, execution and monitoring of public policies. The mentioned decree also lists
with considerable detail specific control and audit responsibilities of the SFC. The new
framework also redefines ties between the SIC and the external control system under the
purview of the TCU.
82.   By way of these new functional definitions, the new internal control framework has
been aligned with other government management initiatives, and in particular with the
objectives of the PPA and the LRF.
83.   Institutional arranaements for  internal control and audit.  The overall
responsibility for the SIC remains with the SFC, which is defined as the central organ of the
system, with normative and technical oversight authority over internal control audit in the
federal Executive Branch. The September 2000 decree also provides for Internal Control
Secretariats (CISET) at the presidency, the attorney general's office, the foreign affairs
ministry and the defense ministry, defining them as sectorial organs. The internal control
units of military commands are defined as sectorial units of the CISET of the ministry of
defense, and the regional internal control units (GRCI) of state governments are defined as
regional units of the SFC.  Both, the SFC and the CISET may establish regional and
sectorial units. A Commission for the Coordination of Internal Control (CCCI), is
responsible for promoting the integration and the harmonization of criteria on matters under
the SCI's purview and for the evaluation of activities under the SCI. The CCI is chaired
by the SFC and its membership includes the heads of the sectorial units. There are



113
important changes in the organization and focus of SFC, partly prompted by the September
2000 decree. From the organizational standpoint (i) all internal control work has been
centralized in five program audit units (Diretorias de Auditoria de Programas), based on
the Executive Branch's main action areas: economic, social, infrastructure, human
resources and administration, and a unit for the management of the SCI (Diretoria de
Gestdo do Sistema de Controle Interno), and (ii) all internal audit units of federal ministries
and agencies in Brasilia have been closed-down and fiscalization resources have been
concentrated in the SFC headquarters in order to streamline operational efficiency and
effectiveness. The SFC is also focusing internal control and audit work on the evaluation
of public policies, in a process based on the analysis of the federal budget from the
perspective of PPA programs, in order to define internal control and audit strategies and
operating plans for each program in terms of types and frequency of fiscalization actions.
While it is perhaps too early to assess this new approach to internal control, it is no doubt
consistent with other major policy developments, in particular the PPA and the LRF.
84.   The SFC is a strong technical body that organizes its operations on the basis of
strategic planning and specific Quarterly control and audit targets for each of its regional
and sectorial units. Table A6.6 provides an indication of the scope of SFC operations since
its creation in 1994.

85.   SFC's fiscalization programs cover a large cross-section of public sector activities,
involving approximately 1,100 auditable government entities and 110 programs funded
with external resources. It has a total staff of approximately 1,400, 85% of which with
audit and fiscalization functions. It is presently in the process of hiring 200 more auditors,
with the aim of fulfilling its estimated total operational requirement of 1,800 to 2,000
employees. While fiscalization activities ranging from formal audits to continuous
monitoring of government actions in key areas, remains the main thrust of the internal
control process, the SFC also plays a role in the restructuring of federal organizations; debt
securitization and debt assumptions by the federal government; evaluation of government



114
programs (which show and increase from 1,556 fiscalization actions in 1995 to 4,952 in
1998); fiscalization of bidding and other procurement processes at the federal, state and
municipal levels; monthly accounting analysis of approximately 600 management units;
integration in SIAFI of the monthly financial statements of states and of municipalities of
state capitals; integration in SIAFI of the annual financial statements of approximately
5,000 municipalities; monitoring and audit of compliance under contracts and agreements
between the federal government and state, municipal and civil society entities receiving
federal funding; monitoring and auditing of government payroll and pension expenditures;
and accounting audits of external loans and grants, where the SFC acts as external auditors
on behalf of international agencies including the World Bank, IADB, U.S. EXIMBANK,
UNDP, KFW, FAO, OIMT and others. On the basis of its fiscalization work, the SFC
monitors and prepares situation reports on 16 sectors or areas: agriculture; federal
administration and state reform; science and technology; communications; culture;
education; fiscal; industry, trade and tourism; justice; environment; mining and energy;
planning and budgeting; social security; health; labor; and transportation.
86.   Information systems used for internal control. SFC operations are supported by
an information system known as ATIVA, used for the planning, administration and control
of the execution of audit and other fiscalization activities. ATIVA is a multifunction
software package developed by SERPRO for the SCF which is used for: managing the
information systems (h/w, s/w, telecommunications and IT resources) of the SFC; plan and
monitor the execution SFC's audit and fiscalization processes; evaluating and monitoring
the performance of audit and fiscalization staff with the support of a specialized subsystem
know as SALVA (for variable logistic evaluation system); providing electronic mail
services to users; producing standardized management reports to monitor accounting results
in government agencies and programs subject to fiscalization; providing on-line
information of legislation relevant o fiscalization processes and the SFC's technical audit
and fiscalization manuals; monitoring governmental human resources processes, including
audit and fiscalization training provided; providing management reports on the results and
certifications granted pursuant to audits and other fiscalization actions. ATIVA is linked
SIAFI. Aside from recording in SIAFI the budgetary and financial execution of the SFC,
ATIVA includes a module (AUDITAR) which performs "paperless" audits, as well as
another module (SIGECONV) and uses SIAFI outputs to audit the compliance of contracts
between the federal government and states, municipalities, foundations and other entities
benefiting from government funding. ATIVA has various other subsystems that support
fiscalization management: SIAR - for the identification and analysis of areas in the federal
government representing internal control risks; WINSAPE - allowing users to schedule
fiscalization and audit staff according to their own requirements; PRESTACONTA -
which supports the automation of the SFC activities, making available audit reports, audit
certifications, audit recommendations and pronouncements by internal control units and
ministries, also supporting the TCU's evaluation of public managers; and, SALVA which
monitors the performance of auditors and other fiscalization staff. From a technological
standpoint ATIVA operates in a mainframe environment serving a network of terminals in
its operating units in Brasilia and 23 state capitals, allowing the monitoring and verification
of SFC activities throughout the country.



115
Generalfiscalization conditions and links between the external and internal control
areas
87.   Conversations with SFC suggest that while the more developed state governments
and their metropolitan municipalities, mostly in South-East of the country, have modern,
strong, and auditable financial management and accounting systems, critical deficiencies
are still observed in these areas in the North-East and the interior, both at the state and the
municipal government levels. This situation can only be redressed over the long term.
However, federal financial management and accounting shows a more positive and
balanced situation and outlook because of the widespread SIAFI and SIDOR networks.
88.   All SFC audit and other fiscalization reports are made available to the TCU, and
also submitted to Congress and federal authorities as required. Conversations with SFC
suggest that there are strong institutional and operational ties with TCU, that is, between the
external and the internal systems.
89.   Though there is no direct linkage between the systems operated by fiscalization
agencies and the budgeting and financial management system of the MOP and the MOF,
SFC auditors have on-line access to the SIAFI and SIDOR systerns for the performance of
their fiscalization fimctions. It is expected that the same type of access will be available in
the future for SIG, the relatively new PPA program management system.

ANNEX 7: DEBT MANAGEMENT INSTITUTrIONS
1.    This annex reviews federal debt trends and debt policies in Brazil, with particular
attention to Brazil's recent innovations regarding debt management institutions. The first section
provides the background on recent trends and policies. The Second section focuses on the issues
and challenges that Brazil's debt management policies are facing. The importance of Brazil's
debt as a policy issue is highlighted by the fact that Brazil's net public sector debt grew from
28.5 percent of GDP in 1995 to 46.5 percent in 2000 and that much of this debt is short term
debt. In addition, a rapidly changing operating environment characterized by increased reliance
on market sources for financing, greater exposure to financial risks resulting from financial
liberalization, and larger international capital flows also places a stronger challenge on debt
management institutions and policies. These challenges became particularly evident by the
international attention paid to Brazil's debt structure as a source of vulnerability in late 1998, and
the decision to shift from exchange rate targeting to inflation targeting in the first half of 1999.
Since March 1999, there has been a rapid evolution in debt policies that is addressing the new
challenges under the new monetary policy environment.
BACKGROUND
2.    By September 2000, net public sector debt had increased to $548 billion (or 48.5 percent
of GDP), from R$ 269 billion in December 1996 (33 percent of GDP). The net debt of the
federal government constituted 29.4 percent of GDP, the net debt of state and local governments
constituted 16.7% percent of GDP (mostly state governments), and that of state-owned
enterprises, 2.4 percent (mostly from public enterprises at the state level. Net public sector debt
is calculated by subtracting liquid financial assets from liabilities in the consolidated balance
sheet of the public sector, which is standard practice when referring to the Brazilian sovereign
debt position.
3.    Net public sector debt is the sum of net internal and external liabilities, where net internal
liabilities include the monetary base. Net internal debt constituted 37.0 percent of GDP, while
net external debt was 9.5 percent. Over time, the share of net public sector domestic debt
relative to net external debt has increased. Domestic debt comprised 66 percent of total debt in
January 1997, but increased to 78 percent in December 1998. Despite the devaluation in January
1999, the relative shares, as of June 2000, have remained at this level.
4.    Gross federal securities outstanding in the domestic market in June 2000, excluding
Treasury securities held by the Central Bank, amounted to R$504.5 billion or 43 percent of
GDP. The total stock of federal securities has increased substantially since 1993, reflecting
increased financing costs due to higher interest rates, assumption of state debts, and
recapitalization of state-owned banks.
5.    The total National Treasury debt, which excludes Central Bank debt, was R$553.4 billion
in June 2000, of which domestic debt was R$425.1 billion (77%) and external debt was R$128.3
billion (23%). The external debt was composed of bonded debt of R$92.6 billion and contractual
debt of R$35.7 billion. The bonded debt included Brady bonds worth R$60.0 billion.
Contractual debt includes loans from multilateral agencies.



117
6.    The following subsections give further background information on debt management
policy since the Real Plan, current institutional and legal framework for debt management, and
current organizational arrangements for debt management.
7.    Debt Management Policy Since the Real Plan. After achieving price stability in 1994,
the Treasury authorities' medium-term objective was to increase the maturity and duration of the
liability portfolio by increasing the share of fixed-coupon securities, but the speculative attack of
October 1997 compelled the Central Bank to raise interest rates substantially, thereby imposing a
capital loss on the holders of longer-maturity fixed-rate debt and consequently reducing demand
for these securities.
8.    After a brief period of issuing floating rate instruments at the end of 1997, the Treasury
was able to resume the issuance of fixed-rate securities as domestic interest rates returned to the
pre-crisis level in the first quarter of 1998, but the Russian crisis in August 1998 provoked a
fuirther, more extended speculative attack, resulting in a return to high interest rates. In response
to these various shocks, the share of fixed-rate instruments declined from 55.6 percent in May
1998 to 3.5 percent of federal securities in the market in December 1998, while floating rate
instruments increased to 69.1 percent and dollar-indexed instruments rose to 21 percent by the
same date. The January 1999 devaluation pushed the latter share to 30.4 percent in January
1999.
9.    The changes in the debt structure by instrument (Table A7. 1) had a substantial effect on
the duration of the portfolio, increasing its exposure interest rate risks. However the refinancing
risk did not increase in similar proportion because the Treasury extended the average maturity of
the portfolio using long-term floaters ( 3 and 5 years).
Table A7.1 - Composition by Instrument of the Domestic Debt: 1993-99
Debt       Foreign   Floating  Inflation  Fixed rate   Other
Outstanding   Exchange    rate    linked      (%)       (%)
(R$ billion)  linked (%)    (%)     (%)
1993          4.9         17.3       3.8      42.1       26.4      10.4
1994          61.7         8.3       16.0      12.5      40.2      23.0
1995         108.4         5.3       37.8      5.3       42.7      8.9
1996         176.2         9.4       18.6      1.8       61.0      9.2
1997         255.5         15.4      34.8      0.3       40.9      8.6
1998         323.8        21.0       69.1      0.3       3.5       6.1
1999         414.9        24.2       61.1      0.3       9.2       5.2
Source: Boletim do Banco Central do Brasil. February 2000. Figures at the end of period.
10.   On the external front, the long period of debt negotiation with the commercial banks and
the Paris Club concluded in 1993, resulting in the issuance of Brady bonds and opening a new
era of relationship with the commercial sources. Since then the government has actively
increased the share of financing from external sources relative to domestic sources, but the
government also continued to issue relatively modest amounts of bonds in key international



118
markets in order to facilitate the access of private issuers to these markets. Like other Latin
borrowers, it has also been involved in the restructuring of its Brady debt.
11.   Institutional and Legal Framework for Debt Management The Central Bank was the
main institution that managed the public external debt in Brazil up until 1986 when the transfer
of debt management responsibilities from the Central Bank to the Treasury began with the
establishment of the STN within the Treasury. Under the new arrangement, the STN was to be
responsible for the administration of both internal and external debt. The Treasury is therefore
legally responsible for making the ultimate decisions regarding the issuance, issue size, maturity,
timing and characteristics of the Treasury securities.
12.   But despite the legal transfer of debt management responsibilities from the Central Bank
to the Treasury, the Central Bank continued to be an important issuer of federal securities in the
domestic market due to its monetary policy objectives, and it continued to influence the
administration of the public debt. In addition, the actual transfer of responsibilities for external
debt management from the Central Bank to the Treasury has proceeded very slowly. The Central
Bank continues to manage the government's access to the international capital market as well as
the dialogue with the lenders involved in the Brady bonds and Paris Club negotiations because of
the complexity and sensitivity of the issues at stake and the existent capacity in the Central Bank.
Nevertheless, the Treasury continues to build its capacity on debt management in order to
eventually assume the responsibilities, but progress has been slow.
13.   Adding to the disperse institutional arrangement, several government agencies are also
involved in the management of loans linked to projects, some of which are from multilateral and
bilateral agencies. Because such loans have different dynamics compared to the typical Treasury
operation in the international markets, currently the Treasury has only a marginal involvement in
these loans. Instead, COFIEX, a committee which has a representative from the Ministry of
Planning as its secretariat, authorizes of these loans.  Because these decisions are made
separately by COFIEX, assessment and authorization of project based loans have a tendency to
be made independent of a financial assessment of the overall debt management policy. At the
operational level, each of the Ministries and the Financial Policy Unit of the Ministry of Finance
act as implementing agencies. They are responsible for budgeting, accounting and payment of
these loans, thus multiplying the number of back offices involve in the management of this debt.
14.   The current legal framework is comprised by a set of decrees and resolutions that
separately deal with the external and internal debt, as described in Box A7.1 below. Existing
legislation lacks a clear statement of objectives for debt management although there are rather
implicit references to some of these objectives in some of these legal vehicles. In particular,
Decree 3366 of 2000 defines the roles of the Treasury and Senate resolution 57/95 authorizes the
government to borrow in the international markets and explicitly allows the government to
borrow externally to pay back domestic debt only if the debt will be cheaper. Nevertheless, there
is little or no explicit reference to cost/risk objectives.



119
Box A7.1 - Legal Framework for Debt Management
Public debt issuance by the National Treasury must be programued in the Federal Budget,
specifically observing the following legislation:
* LFR - Lei de Responsabilidade Fiscal (Law of Fiscal Responsibility), establishes long term
fiscal sustainability planning.
* LDO - Lei de Diretrizes Oreamentarias (Budget Guidelines Law), establishes guidelines for
debt issuance.
* LOA - Lei Orcamentaria Anual (Annual Budget Law), sets the debt issuance ceiling.
The legal framework is defined separately between external and domestic debt.
For external debt the legal framework is defined by:
* Resolution SF no 57/95 and 51/97, set the limits (USS10 billion) and conditions for issuing
bonds (proceeds must be used to retire internal debt) in the international capital markets.
*  Resolution SF no. 69/96, authorizes buyback and structured traw tions involving external
debt.
For domestic debt the legal fmework is defined by:
* Medida Provisoria no. 1697-57/29.07.98, describes general terms and conditions of the
public debt instruments manaWed by the National Treasury.
* Decreto no. 2701/30.07.98, establishes the characteristics of each instrunent issued by the
Treasury.
Partaria no. 189 de 03/08/98, delegates to the Treasury Secretary the auority for debt issuance redemption and
debt swaps.
15.   Oreanizational Arrangements.  Currently the organizational arrangement for debt
management is split between two government institutions, the STN within the Ministry of
Finance and the Central Bank (Table A7.2). The relevant divisions within the STN are as
follows:
* General Coordination of Foreign Affairs (COREX) - in charge of external debt
* General Coordination of Public Debt Management (CODIP) - in charge of domestic debt
* General Coordination of Contingent Liabilities Restructuring (COARP) - in charge of
contingent liabilities
The organizational structure of the STN has instances of overlap. For example, each of the
external and internal departments has an operational or back office unit and a unit responsible for
analytical work. In addition, the structure could benefit from more specialization in the tasks
typically performed by a modem treasury organization.
16.  In the Central Bank the relevant departments in debt management are:
* Department of Open Market Operations (DEMAB) - conduct of monetary policy and
domestic debt issuance
* Foreign Capital Department (FIRCE) - negotiation of new external borrowing



120
* International Relations Department (DERIN) - negotiations of restructuring of external
borrowing
In addition, the Central Bank manages international reserves through:
* Department of International Reserves Operations (DEPIN)
17.    The Central Bank executes transactions for the Treasury, including auctions for new
issues, and payments of principal and interest. It also evaluates proposals for debt restructuring,
such as buy-back programs, and acts as the front office permanently in communication with the
market, receiving proposals from investment banks. The Central Bank is also the manager of the
SELIC, or the Electronic Clearance and Custody System, through which Treasury auctions are
carried out and settled.40  In effect, the Central Bank performs the settlement functions for all
liabilities, as well as front, middle and back office functions for all external liabilities. The
Central Bank also manages foreign reserves.

transactions of Central Bank and Treasury instruments in secondary market
18.    One important aspect of the current organizational structure is the limited presence of a
middle office which typically is responsible for analyzing the debt portfolio structure. The
portfolio analysis and planning activities that do take place in Brazil appear to be relatively
limited in scope, piecemeal, ad hoc, and limited in circulation. The underlying talent and
knowledge of many of the professionals in both the STN and the Central Bank, however, suggest
that the basic capabilities are in place to allow significant progress to be achieved, even within
existing institutional arrangements. The limited nature of current middle-office type activity
40 See Barbosa (1997) "Public Debt Management: The Recent Brazilian Experience 1995-1997", STN.



121
within the Brazilian debt management is one of the issues that recent reforms are beginning to
address.
ISSUES
19.   The current structure of the sovereign debt exposes the general budget to financial risks
and is a source of fiscal vulnerability to the economy that requires special attention from debt
managers and policy makers. Addressing these risks and vulnerability requires a professional
and comprehensive approach to debt management that involves changes in the policy framework
and in the institutional and analytical framework, as well as a substantial upgrade of debt
management capabilities in the STN.
20.   Policy framework. In order to achieve medium-term stability, a debt management
objective should be to minimize the volatility of the real interest rate paid on the debt, since it is
this parameter which most directly affects debt dynamics. Any benchmark that is developed
relevant for the next few years is likely to have this objective at its core. Large, unanticipated
increases in real interest payments will be perceived as forcing the government away from its
medium-term debt: GDP trajectory. Unanticipated increases in the real interest bill will also
have an immediate impact on other elements of domestic expenditure, which are already under
stress. Little relief is to be expected from the current government revenue-raising capacity
constraints.
21.   With a high percentage of domestic debt maturing in the short term and a high proportion
of floating rate securities relative to fixed rate securities, pursuit of the objective described above
means moving toward a more even distribution of maturities and an increased use of fixed-rate
instruments. A situation where the government fails to rollover the debt or, more accurately,
faces an unacceptably high price for refinancing at an inopportune moment can lead to financial
vulnerability. Accordingly, quantifying and managing refinancing risk is a priority, even though,
as the authorities point out, refinancing debt in the domestic market has not so far been a
problem. The point is rather that the concentration of maturities is seen by markets as a sign of
vulnerability which becomes an element in the country risk premium and raises funding costs.
Also the costs of any perceived failure to rollover debt are large, a situation that must be avoided.
22.   The normal mechanism for dealing with refinancing risk at an acceptable cost is to issue
medium- and long-term floating rate bonds. The maturity could be extended even if duration is
not, by testing the market for longer-dated (3, 5 and 7-year) floating-rate instruments. The
underlying goal must be to deal with the refinancing and interest rate risks one at a time, by first
reducing refinancing risk to a more tolerable level and thereafter reducing the interest rate risk.
By issuing large issues, the increase in liquidity can compensate for the premium demanded by
the market for longer dated securities. A concurrent program to change the reset interval for the
coupon, from the current 1 day to 3 months, and then to 6 months, could be implemented to
extend duration.
23.   In addition to the repricing risk at rollover on fixed-rate instruments, interest rate risk is
also significant on a continuous basis, as there has been an increasing concentration of floating
rate instruments in the current debt structure. The floating rate instruments, particularly the
LFTs (Letras Financeiras do Tesouro, issued by the Treasury) and LBCs (Letras do Banco
Central, issued by the Central Bank), which are indexed to the overnight interest rate (SELIC),



122
are very sensitive to changes in interest rates. While high frequency adjustments may have made
sense for investors during periods of high and variable inflation, this is not the economic
environment any longer. By offering fixed rate instruments, the government could share the risk
with market participants who have different risk preferences, thereby benefiting both parties.
Another option for reducing the interest rate risk is to increase the proportion of coupon bonds
and amortization bonds and reduce the number of discounted bullet bonds. This can smooth the
debt-service cash flow and reduce interest rate risk as well as refinancing risk.
24.   The government has, to date, excluded the option to issue inflation-indexed securities,
partly due to low appetite for these securities, and partly for fear of reversing the general success
in deindexing the economy, or signaling to the market that the economy is heading towards
higher inflation in the future. Nevertheless, the Government does not rule out developing this
market in the future. Indeed, many countries with recent inflationary histories, such as Israel and
Chile, as well as countries with very low and stable inflation like the USA, have been using this
instrument to address the longer term markets. Concerns for adverse signaling can be addressed
by establishing explicit limits on the use of this instrument, for example a maximum percentage
of total debt outstanding, or exclusive use for only the longest tenures (more than 5 years).
25.   On the other hand, dollar-indexed securities have been skillfully used to extend the
maturity of the domestic portfolio. However, the overall exposure to foreign exchange risk in
the consolidated federal debt should probably not be increased any further. With devaluation the
share of federal debt exposed to currency movements has already risen to about 50 percent. The
benefits of maintaining current levels (extended maturity and reduced refinancing risk) need to
be weighed against the risk of exposure to foreign exchange volatility. The objective of
extending the maturity of the debt profile through the use of dollar-linked securities should be
maintained and efforts in this direction need be strengthened but without increasing the overall
exposure.
26.   In addition to improvements in the public debt structure, the government can reduce the
cost and risk of the public debt portfolio over the medium run by fuirther developing the domestic
bond market. This is particularly important for Brazil given the importance of the domestic debt
stock both as a share of debt and as a source of vulnerability, and it also follows international
trend towards a greater use of domestic currency debt as a public debt risk management device.
27.   The government can play a crucial role in the development of the domestic debt market
as the largest participant in the market, and as a provider of liquidity and a benchmark yield
curve that will facilitate the pricing of securities in the primary and secondary markets.
Government actions which can improve primary and secondary markets operations are as
follows: pre-announce auctions, standardize and increase the fungibility of instruments, phase
out Central Bank primary issues, promote market making activities, and promote the creation of
centralized electronic trading system. Some of these proposals have already been partially
implemented by the government. Debt managers will need to ensure that their policies and
operations are consistent with the development of an efficient government securities market.



123
28.   Governance and Institutional Framework. The governance framework required for
sound sovereign debt management in Brazil should seek the right mixture of incentives and
accountability for government debt managers. A clear set of objectives for managing the
government's debt, a satisfactory cost and risk framework, specific portfolio and risk
management policies, and further centralization of the government debt management
organization are principles which were the basis for the recent reforms of the debt management
governance and institutional framework in several countries.
29.   An optimal governance framework is grounded in a clear legal framework that clarify
the authority to borrow and to issue new debt, invest, and undertake transactions on the
government's behalf.  The authority to borrow is clearly defined by legislation.  Sound
governance practices are an important component of sovereign debt management, particularly for
countries like Brazil which have large government debt portfolios.
30.   The soundness and credibility of the financial system can be supported by assurances that
the government debt portfolio is being managed prudently and efficiently.  Moreover,
counterparts need assurances that the sovereign debt managers have the legal authority to
represent the government, and that the government stands behind any transactions its sovereign
debt managers enter into. An important feature of the legal framework is the authority to issue
new debt, which is normally stipulated in the form of either borrowing authority legislation with
a preset limit or a debt ceiling.
31.   Centralization of authority to issue new debt complemented by mechanisms which
prevent the potential over-issuance of debt are critical features of the legal framework of debt
management. Brazilian authorities have been moving towards implementing these principles
into their legal framework. For example, the Law of Fiscal Responsibility prevents the Central
Bank from issuing new debt, restricting their activities to repos and the secondary market. This
represents a further move towards centralizing the authority to issue debt in the National
Treasury. In addition, proposals for placing limits on the amount of new debt are currently being
discussed by the government.
32.   For project based loans, Brazilian authorities could assess the need for further
consolidation and centralization because currently the management of these loans is spread
across several agencies outside of the National Treasury. One can argue that while the front
office operations for these loans could be handled by the Ministries under which the projects are
managed given the unique dynamics of project based loans, there seems to be room for further
consolidation in the National Treasury of the back office operations such as budgeting,
accounting, and payment. The advantage of such a consolidation is reduced operational risk and
improved risk management.
33.   The main obstacle to further consolidation is the need for more personnel and
organizational support in the National Treasury necessary to handle the increase in workload.
Possibly an assessment needs to be made of where unnecessary overhead can be simplified and
reduced before centralizing the back office operations of the project based loans. This would
reduce the need for additional manpower and organizational structure that would result from
such a consolidation.



124
34.   Another area which the Brazilian authorities could potentially improve are explicit
regulations concerning trading of options and derivatives in the domestic market. Options and
derivative have become standard tools for risk management for sovereign debt management
authorities in developed countries.  In Brazil, the authority to operate in the options and
derivatives domestic market is currently lacking in the legal framework.
35.   The organizational framework for debt management needs to be well specified, and
ensure that mandates and roles are well articulated. Legal arrangements work best when
supported by delegation of appropriate authority to debt managers. Experience suggests that
there is a range of institutional alternatives for allocating sovereign debt management functions
across one or more agencies, including in one or more of the following: the Ministry of Finance,
Central Bank, autonomous Debt Management Agency, and Central Depository. Regardless of
which approach is chosen, the key requirement is to ensure that the organizational framework
surrounding debt management be clearly specified, that there be coordination and sharing of
information, and that the mandates of the respective players be clear.
36.   In order to further ensure that the debt management organizational framework is clearly
specified in Brazil, a draft of a management and operations guideline which defines functions
and responsibilities within the National Treasury, and particularly for the Debt Office, has been
prepared. The guideline also defines the interrelationship among the various offices within the
Treasury. The Ministry of Planning is currently revising the document. The next step would be
to develop detailed operations manual for individual positions within the Treasury.
37.   Many debt managers file an annual debt management report, which reviews the previous
year's activities, and provides a broad overview of borrowing plans for the current year based on
the annual budget projections. These reports increase the accountability of the government debt
managers. They also assist financial markets by disclosing the criteria used to guide the debt
program, the assumptions and trade-offs underlying the setting of these criteria, and the
managers' performance in meeting them.
38.   As part of the new governance structure, it is also important that there is a clear definition
of responsibilities between the part of the government responsible for approving and supervising
the primary decisions on the public debt management and the other part responsible for
executing that policy in a efficient and transparent way.
39.   Clear debt management objectives are essential in order to reduce uncertainty as to the
government's willingness to trade off cost and risk. Unclear objectives often lead to poor
decisions on how to manage the existing debt and what types of debt to issue, particularly during
times of market instability, resulting in a potentially risky and expensive debt portfolio for the
government and adding to its vulnerability to a crisis. Lack of clarity with respect to objectives
also creates uncertainty within the financial community. This can increase government debt
servicing costs because investors incur costs in attempting to monitor and interpret the
government's objectives and policy framework, and may require a higher risk premium because
of this uncertainty.
40.   Today, governments (treasuries) typically run an integrated operation for the external and
domestic debt. This replaces the earlier arrangement where a Treasury unit, under the Ministry



125
of Finance, was responsible for managing the local currency debt, and the Central Bank was
responsible for managing the external debt.
41.   In some countries, this integration of the external and domestic operations under the
Ministry of Finance has been further modified by strengthening the independence of the resulting
combined operation. This innovation was inspired in most cases by the need to separate the
accountability for the choice of debt policy (expressed in the benchmark) from the accountability
for risk management and operations. It has also been stimulated by the need to attract and retain
highly skilled individuals with an understanding of the financial risk analysis in order to perform
the more sophisticated debt management operations generated by the framework mentioned
above. An additional reason sometimes mentioned is the need to transfer the operational risks of
the more active debt management to an independent vehicle away from government bureaucracy.
Governments which first took the decision to establish an independent debt management office
were Ireland and Sweden, followed by New Zealand and Belgium and more recently Portugal.
42.   The other important organizational need is the establishment of a unified and formal
middle office. A middle office provides the research, analysis, decision support information and
planning activities that are necessary and useful to senior management and the portfolio decision
makers. In the past, the management units in the Treasury or in the Ministry of Finance were
dominated initially by a back office structure that then evolved into a front/back office operation
when the markets became a major source of funding.  With the shift towards a more
sophisticated framework of risk analysis and an integrated portfolio, the need for more formality
in the analysis forced the institutions to incorporate in their organization a middle office
function. This development provided support to policymakers in making decisions based on a
rigorous market risk analysis rather than the traditional heuristic approach.
43.   To proceed with the creation of a competent middle office unit the following issues need
to be considered:
*  A small but fully dedicated analytical team to develop and lead the project should be
organized. The head of this team should be a highly qualified individual with a strong
technical background that will provide intellectual leadership to the exercise and win the
credibility of the technical teams in the Central Bank and Treasury.
* A decision to establish a unified middle office unit in the Treasury (as opposed to the current
arrangement of operations divided between the Treasury and the Central Bank) would be
desirable. Formalizing the existence of the technical group from the beginning will be better
than just having an ad-hoc group but, in either case, having a clear mandate from the top is a
prerequisite.
* Define duties and overall objectives of the middle office. This will help establish
responsibility for centralized risk monitoring and management, as well as the independence
and authority for managing risk, to make sure that longer-term risks and potential costs are
not ignored in pursuit of short-term budgetary savings. (note: does this mean that the middle
office is responsible for managing as well as monitoring risk?)
* Ensure that the middle office has sufficient autonomy to do its job effectively by ensuring
that the debt management office has the information systems, authorities and accountabilities
in place to implement strategies successfully.



126
* The middle office needs to be supported by an integrated information system that would
support the front, middle and back office functions of debt management. Work should begin
by building upon existing systems to facilitate analysis and then to work over the medium
term to modernize the system as a whole.
Box A7.2 provides the major areas of responsibility for a middle office, as well as some of the
critical success factors.

44.   The core message is that domestic and foreign liabilities should be managed in a
common, integrated framework such that portfolio and balance sheet risk can be assessed as a
whole. This is the underlying reason for establishing a common middle office structure. Such a
middle office structure supports a decision-making process that permits vulnerability, risk and
cost to be more judiciously assessed in sovereign balance sheet management. It allows the
government to develop a structure of asset and liabilities that would protect the public finances
under a range of domestic and international economic scenarios, and to sketch a transition path
toward that structure by designing a medium-term funding strategy.
45.   Irrespective of the institutional setting adapted, the government must be able to enforce
accountability for delivering high quality sovereign debt management and prevent activities
which can add considerable risk to the government's balance sheet. In addition to the general
principles outlined at the beginning of this subsection, specific actions can be taken in order to
strengthen accountability:
* Establish a risk management office within the debt office with the responsibility for
monitoring and reporting on all portfolio and operational risk



127
* Authorize an outside body of advisors to provide oversight and advice across a broad
range of operational and strategic risk management issues.
*  Ensure appropriate auditing of the financial transactions undertaken by the debt office
in order to enforce compliance with the portfolio management policies
*  Establish a code of conduct for the debt managers.
46.   The enforcement of accountability is designed to foster a strong risk management culture.
A code of conduct which provides guidance on matters such as personal portfolios, acceptance of
gifts and representation can similarly encourage behavior standards which can complement the
above activities in ensuring prudent management of the government debt. One of the most
critical responsibilities of the head of the debt office is to enforce proper ethical standards and
foster a strong risk management culture. One possible mechanism for dealing with ethical issues
is to establish an Ethical Committee inside the National Treasury.
47.   Currently in Brazil, a draft of a code of conduct for the Treasury has been prepared which
contain many of the issues mentioned above concerning the conduct of debt management
officials. The draft has been submitted to the legal council within the National Treasury, and
subsequently will be submitted to the Minister of Finance for his approval.
48.   Analytical framework The analytical framework for debt management is based on the
fact that the external and internal debt portfolios are increasingly managed as linked parts of an
overall public debt stock, since they together meet the government's funding needs. The
currency of issue, although an important feature, is no longer considered a sufficient reason for
isolated analysis and management, particularly as foreign investors increasingly hold domestic
debt. An integrated approach to debt management has enabled debt managers to focus on
broader strategic policies to reduce risk over the medium term, including the development of the
domestic debt market. Managing separate parts of the portfolio independently can produce
short-term efficiencies but at the expense of the larger strategic goal of managing risk.
49.   The funding strategy should emphasize risk management and long-term cost reductions,
specifying for instance the issuance plan for the next three to five years -- how much is to be
borrowed, how to borrow, what debt instruments to issue or how to sell them. The strategy
should lay out a specific plan for the transition from floating rate debt to long-term fixed rate
instruments. This plan needs to prioritize the management of different risks and appropriately
deal with the cost-risk trade off. Specifically, in the order of severity, the sovereign debt risks
Brazil faces currently are refinancing risk, interest rate risk, and exchange risk. In terms of cost,
extending the maturity of fixed rate instruments over a short period is costly and should be done
gradually.
50.   A component of the new challenge is the need to develop the analytical tools which
allows for a more active debt management. This implies defining, in a more systematic way, the
desirable debt portfolio structure and then taking concrete actions to achieve the set parameters.
This process includes a more explicit concern for risk, an explicit definition of the risk tolerance
level, and therefore the need to find the appropriate balance in the trade-off between risk and
cost. The ex ante definition of the risk tolerance level in turn allows authorities to make more
precise estimates of the impact of debt service on the budget. Active debt management should
therefore use a framework such as strategic benchmarks that requires the authority to quantify



128
the risks, define limits to those risks, and in many cases, express the desired portfolio in a form
which incorporates the desired risk/cost trade-off.

which focused on the quality of projections and the analytical tools of risk analysis, but the
analysis was done within a static context. The next step would be do the assessment within a
dynamic model which allows scenario analysis based on changes in funding strategy, extem al
conditions, and macroeconomic parameters. Such a model would be critical in improving risk
management through optimal funding strategy.
53.   In the process of incorporating risk management techniques several countries have found
that the level of rigor is enhanced by looking at the sovereign balance sheet as a whole. This
asset-liability management (ALM) approach follows similar applications in corporate finance,
where it is customary to assess and hedge against the impact of fsancial shocks on the net worth
of the firm. Not surprisingly, these techniques have been more popular in countries that were
more exposed to risk, such as Ireland, Sweden, Belgium and New Zealand. These countries
were followed closely bys similuch as Italy, Deons ark, Finlate   and,
Portugal.
54.   The most recent improvements are related to modeling tools.  The most important
development is the use of statistical software packages that allow multiple scenarios to be
explored in a very cost-efficient way (e.g., Monte-Carlo simulations). This has also facilitated



129
in-house analysis of value-at-risk and cash-flow at risk. The stress scenario is a tool that can be
used when the historical data are not representative of the current regime, and where changes in
policy regime are a possibility. By modeling extreme scenarios, these models help raise
awareness of the circumstances under which the debt service could become unmanageable. It
also allows the government to evaluate different funding strategies that senior officials need to
consider in order to achieve the desired portfolio structure.
55.   Capacitv building. For governments in emerging markets such as Brazil, debt
management has usually been a very basic operation until recently. Debt management has been
very back office oriented (debt registration, disbursements, and debt service payments), with
limited strategic and financial analysis carried out by the debt management team. Strategic
thinking and key funding decisions were made much more at the macro level, close to the
minister's office or in the Central Bank, guided by fiscal and balance of payment considerations.
56.   However, recent developments has resulted in a situation where the lack of strategic
thinking is a major constraint on sovereign debt management, and strategic thinking must be
supported by upgraded analytical capacity and market skills. Priority should be given to
improving these capacities when expanding operational capacity in the debt office.
57.   When evaluating the need to build strong operational capacity, there are two areas that
require special attention: human resources and information technology (IT) infrastructure.
58.   The debt management organization will need to be properly staffed. With the new
emphasis on market operation and risk management, debt management will require new skills.
The demand for new skills could be addressed by retraining existing staff, and by bringing
resources from other areas of the government or the market-such as the Central Bank, public
banks, and leading public export companies-which are more routinely exposed to these
disciplines.
59.   The skills required in the middle office to analyze risk and cost/risk tradeoffs will
probably be the most difficult resources to obtain inside the public sector or even in the local
market. In such a situation, the process of building capacity will need to be strongly supported
with significant investment in training and knowledge transfer. At a later stage, if the
government decides to engage in active management of the debt portfolio for purposes of
mitigating risk exposure, the proper skills will also be needed to avoid an increase in operational
risks resulting from activities such as swaps and options.
60.   In most countries, the central government faces serious limitations in properly
compensating specialized human resources. The need to attract and retain personnel with the
proper skills is one of the important reasons why governments establish an independent DMO.
Addressing this issue properly should be an important policy issue when trying to build debt
management capability.
61.   In addition to human resources, a good organization needs an information technology
infrastructure in order to ensure timely supply of accurate and comprehensive information of its
core activities. The debt management operation needs basic information on the portfolio (stocks
and flows), the relevant financial variables, and transaction processing. The basic information



130
needs to be processed in order to conduct analysis of the debt, risk exposure of the portfolio, and
developments in markets where the debt instruments are being traded.
62.   In terms of the information system, the evolution has been similar to the changes in the
organizational structure, in the sense that typically the system was designed mainly for the back
office environment. However, the new needs arising from middle office analysis have been
supported by the development of a parallel system based on PCs and in-house software, at first
without any integration with the existing back office information system. The trend towards
integrated systems has accelerated recently, with commercial banks taking the lead. In the case
of sovereign debt management, derivatives are important tools for the active management of the
debt structure, and an information system that could support these tools must be in place in order
to manage the operational risks of debt management.
63.   In general, efforts are likely to continue to further enhance the operational capacity of the
debt management office in the STN, particularly for the front office which is responsible for
dealing with the market. Strong skills in internal and external market operations need to be
consolidated. Risk management capacity needs to be established in the middle office of the STN
which would include an integrated ALM framework to analyze risk, develop analytical tools to
conduct rigorous analysis, and provide debt reports and information to the public.



131
